A! benedicite! for al among that fare  
The harm is doon, and fare-wel feldfare!"

124

"And, nece myn, ne take it not agreef.  
If that ye suffre him al night in this wo,  
God help me so, ye hadde him never leef.  
That dar I seyn, now there is but we two;  
But wel I woot, that ye wol not do so;  
Ye been to wys to do so gret folye,  
To putte his lif al night in jupartye."

125

"Hadde I him never leef? By God, I wene  
Ye hadde never thing so leef," quod she.  
"Now by my thrift," quod he, "that shal be sene;  
For, sin ye make this ensample of me,  
If I al night wolde him in sorwe see,  
For al the tresour in the toun of Troye,  
I bidde God, I never mote have joye!"

126

"Now loke thanne, if ye, that been his love,  
Shul putte al night his lyf in jupartye  
For thing of nought! Now, by that God above,  
Nought only this delay comth of folye,  
But of malyce, if that I shal nought lye.  
What, platly, and ye suffre him in distresse,  
Ye neither bountee doon ne gentilesse!"

127

Quod tho Criseyde, "Wole ye doon o thing,  
And ye therwith shal stinte al his disese;  
Have here, and bereth him this blewe ring.  
For ther is no-thing mighte him bettre plese,  
Save I myself, ne more his herte apese;  
And sey my dere herte, that his sorwe  
Is causeles, that shal be seen to-morwe."

128

"A ring?" quod he, "Ye, hasel-wodes shaken!  
Ye, nece myn, that ring moste han a stoon  
That mighte dede men alyve maken;  
And swich a ring, trowe I that ye have noon.  
Discrecioun out of your heed is goon;  
That fele I now," quod he, "and that is routhe;  
O tyme y-lost, wel maystow cursen slouthe!"

129

"Wot ye not wel that noble and heigh corage  
Ne sorweth not, ne stinteth eek for lyte?  
But if a fool were in a jalous rage,  
I nolde setten at his sorwe a myte.  
But feffe him with a fewe wordes whyte  
Another day, whan that I mighte him finde:  
But this thing stont al in another kinde."

130

"This is so gentil and so tendre of herte,  
That with his deeth he wol his sorwes wreke;  
For trusteth wel, how sore that him smerte,  
He wol to yow no jalouse wordes speke.  
And for-thy, nece, er that his herte breke,  
So spek your-self to him of this matere;  
For with o word ye may his herte stere."

131

"Now have I told what peril he is inne,  
And his coming unwist is to every wight;  
Ne, pardee, harm may ther be noon ne sinne;  
I wol my-self be with yow al this night.  
Ye knowe eek how it is your owne knight,  
And that, by right, ye moste upon him triste,  
And I al prest to fecche him whan yow liste."

132

This accident so pitous was to here,  
And eek so lyk a sooth, at prime face;  
And Troilus hir knight to hir so dere,  
His privet coming, and the siker place,  
That, though that she dide him as thanne a grace,  
Considered alle thinges as they stode.  
No wonder is, sin she dide al for gode.

133

Criseyde answerde, "As wicly God at reste  
My sowle bringe, as me is for him wo!  
And eem, y-wis, fayn wolde I doon the beste.  
If that I hadde grace to do so.  
But whether that ye dwelle or for him go,  
I am, til God me better minde sende,  
At Dulcarnon, right at my wittes ende."

134

Quod Pandarus, "Ye, nece, wol ye here?  
Dulcarnon called is 'fleming of wreches';  
It semeth hard, for wreches wol not lere  
For verray slouthe or othere wilful tecches;  
This seyd by hem that be not worth two feches.  
But ye ben wys, and that we han on honde  
Nis neither hard, ne skilful to withstonde."

135

"Thanne, eem," quod she, "Doth her-of as yow list;  
But er he come I wil up first aryse;  
And, for the love of God, sin al my trist  
Is on yow two, and ye ben bothe wyse,  
So wircheth now in so discreet a wyse,  
That I honour may have, and he plesaunce;  
For I am here al in your govemaunce."

136

"That is wel seyd," quod he, "My nece dere,  
Ther good thrift on that wyse gentil herte!  
But liggeth stille, and taketh him right here.  
It nedeth not no ferther for him sterte;  
And ech of yow ese otheres sorwes smerte.  
For love of God; and, Venus, I thee herie;  
For sone hope I we shulle ben alle merie."

137

This Troilus ful sone on knees him sette  
Ful sobrely, right by hir beddes heed,  
And in his beste wyse his lady grette;  
But lord, so she wex sodeynliche reed!  
Ne, though men sholden smyten of hir heed,  
She coude nought a word aright out-bringe  
So sodeynly, for his sodeyn cominge.

138

But Pandarus, that so wel coude fele  
In every thing, to pleye anoon bigan,  
And seyde, "Nece, see how this lord can knele!  
Now, for your trouthe, seeth this gentil man!"  
And with that word he for a cushion ran,  
And seyde, "Kneleth now, whyl that yow leste,  
Ther God your hertes bringe sone at reste!"

139

Can I not seyn, for she bad him not rise,  
If sorwe it putte out of hir remembraunce,  
Or elles if she toke it in the wyse  
Of duttee, as for his observaunce;  
But wel finde I she dide him this plesaunce,  
That she him kiste, al-though she syked sore;  
And bad him sitte adoun withouten more.

140

Quod Pandarus, "Now wol ye wel beginne;  
Now doth him sitte, gode nece dere,  
Upon your beddes syde al there withinne,  
That ech of yow the bet may other here."  
And with that word he drow him to the fere,  
And took a light, and fond his contenaunce  
As for to loke upon an old romance.

---

### Modern English (Cleaned Translation / Editorial Rendering)

And all her women, by ordinance,  
Were abed, as I have said;  
There was nothing more to skip or to dance,  
But they were bid to go to bed, with mischance.  
If any person was stirring anywhere,  
Let those in bed sleep late and be unaware.

100

But Pandarus, who could well every part  
Of the old dance and every point therein,  
When he saw that everything was right,  
Determined to begin his work forthwith;  
He softly unpinned the stew-door,  
And, still as a stone, without any further hindrance,  
Sat himself down by Troilus.

101

And, shortly to the point:  
Of all this business he told him word and end,  
And said, "Make yourself ready right away;  
For you shall go to heaven's bliss."  
"Now blissful Venus, grant me grace,"  
Quoth Troilus, "for never have I had such need,  
Nor ever the half of this dread before."

102

Said Pandarus, "Fear not in the least,  
For it shall be just as you desire;  
So thrive I, this night I will make it well,  
Or cast all the horror into the fire."  
"Yet blissful Venus, this night inspire me,"  
Quoth Troilus, "as truly as I serve you;  
And ever better and better shall be, till I die."

103

"And if I had, O Venus, full of mirth,  
Bad aspects of Saturn or of Mars,  
Or if you had been quenched or hindered at my birth,  
Pray to your father that he might turn aside this harm,  
And grant that I may once again be glad,  
For the love of him whom you loved in the wood,  
I mean Adonis, who was slain by the boar."

104

"O Jove, you lover of fair Europa,  
Who in the form of a bull carried her away,  
Now help! O Mars, who bears the bloody cloak,  
For love of Venus, hinder me not I pray!  
O Phoebus, recall how Daphne once pined,  
And beneath the bark changed to a laurel:  
Help me now, for love of her so true!"

105

"O Mercury, I beg in Herse's name,  
Though Pallas was angry at Aglauros,  
Now help! Diana, let not modest shame  
Dissuade you from aiding me and abetting!  
O Fatal Sisters, ere my nurse made yet  
My swaddling clothes, you spun my destiny—  
So help me in this work that is begun!"

106

Said Pandar, "O, you chicken-hearted wretch!  
Are you afraid because you think she'll bite?  
Put something on—this over-cloak—just fetch it,  
And follow me to see a sight!  
But wait; I'll go ahead to make all right!"  
Then he undid a little trap-door,  
And brought Troilus in by the lap.

107

The stormy wind so loudly roared about  
That no other sound could anywhere be heard;  
And those whose beds stood near the outer door  
Slept soundly and not a single person stirred.  
Pandarus went to the door at once, with a sober face,  
Where they lay, and he softly shut it tight.

108

He came back again, very still and stealthily,  
But Cressida awoke and cried, "Who goes there?"  
"Dear niece," he softly said, "it is only me;  
Do not be startled, have no fear."  
And near he came, whispering into her ear,  
"No word, for love of God I beseech you;  
Let no one rise and hear of our speech."

109

"What! Which way have you come, benedicite?"  
She said, "And how is it that they know nothing?"  
"Here, at this secret trap-door," he answered.  
Then Cressida said, "Let me call someone."  
"Ah! God forbid that it should happen,"  
Said Pandarus, "that you should act such folly!  
They might suppose things they never before thought."

110

"It is not good to wake a sleeping hound,  
Nor give any cause for suspicion;  
Your women sleep all, I undertake,  
And will sleep until the sun shall shine.  
And when my tale has been told to an end,  
Unseen, just as I came, so will I go."

111

"Now, my niece, you shall well understand,"  
Said he, "as you women all judge,  
That to keep a man in love within your hand,  
And call him her 'lief' and 'dear heart,'  
And make him a boast above his rank—  
I mean, as love one with another in this while—  
She does herself a shame and him a deceit."

112

"Now why do I tell you all this?  
You know yourself, as well as any person,  
That all your love is fully granted  
To Troilus, the worthiest knight,  
One of this world, and furthermore pledged in troth;  
That, unless it were his sole fault, you would not  
Fail him while you live."

113

"Now it stands thus, that since I went from you,  
This Troilus, plainly to say,  
Has crept through a gutter, by a private way,  
Into my chamber in all this rain,  
Unknown to every kind of person, certainly,  
Save me myself, as truly I have joy.  
And by that faith I shall proclaim the fame of Troy!"

114

"And he has come in such pain and distress  
That, unless he be completely mad from this,  
He must suddenly fall into madness,  
Unless God helps; and the cause of this is  
He says he has been told, by a friend of his,  
That you should love one named Horaste,  
For sorrow of which this night shall be his last."

115

Cressida, who heard all this wonder,  
Suddenly felt her heart grow cold.  
And with a sigh she sorrowfully answered,  
"Alas! I thought, whatever tales were told,  
My dear heart would not hold me so lightly false!  
Alas! what wrong conceits these are.  
What harm they do—now I seem to live too long!"

116

"Horaste! Alas! and false to Troilus?  
I know him not, God help me so," she said;  
"Alas! what wicked spirit told him thus?  
Now certainly, tomorrow, if I see him,  
I shall fully excuse myself of that,  
As any woman would, if it please him;"  
And with that word she sighed sorely.

117

"O God!" she cried, "such worldly falseness,  
Which scholars call false felicity,  
Is mingled with much bitterness!  
Full of anguish is the condition, God knows," she said,  
"Of empty prosperity;  
For either joys do not come together,  
Or else no one has them always here."

118

"O fragile welfare of man's joy unstable!  
Of men thou makest but a sport and play!  
All know that they are able to keep their joy,  
Or do not know it — there is no other way.  
Now if one knows it not, how may he say  
That he perceives the spark of perfect joy,  
If ignorance still leaves him in the dark?"

119

"But if he knows that joy is transitory,  
As every joy of worldly things must flee,  
Then every time he who has it in memory,  
The dread of losing it makes that he  
May be in no perfect happiness.  
And if to lose his joy he sets a mite,  
Then it seems that joy is worth very little."

120

"Wherefore I will define in this matter,  
That truly, for all I can see,  
There is no true welfare in this world here.  
But O, thou wicked serpent Jealousy,  
Thou misbelieving and envious folly,  
Why hast thou made Troilus distrust me,  
Who never yet wronged him, as I knew?"

121

Said Pandarus, "Thus fallen is this case."  
"Why, my uncle," she said, "who told him this?  
Why does my dear heart act so, alas?"  
"You know, my niece," he said, "what it is;  
I hope all shall be well that is amiss.  
For you may quench all this, if it please you.  
And do so, for I hold it the best."

122

"So shall I do tomorrow, truly," she said,  
"And God before, so that it shall suffice."  
"Tomorrow? Alas, that would be a mock," he said,  
"No, no, it may not stand in this fashion;  
For, my niece, thus write wise clerks,  
That peril increases with delaying;  
No, such delays are not worth a straw."

123

"Niece, everything has its time, I dare avow;  
For when a chamber is afire, or a hall,  
More need is to rescue suddenly  
Than to dispute and ask among all,  
How did this candle come to fall in the straw?  
Ah! benedicite! for among such dealings  
The harm is done, and fare thee well, fieldfare!"

124

"And, my niece, do not take it as grief.  
If you suffer him all night in this woe,  
God help me so, you would never have him dear.  
That I dare say, now there is but we two;  
But well I know that you will not do so;  
You are too wise to commit so great folly,  
To put his life all night in jeopardy."

125

"Would I have never loved him? By God, I think  
You never had anything so dear," she said.  
"Now by my thrift," he said, "that shall be seen;  
For, since you make this example of me,  
If I all night would suffer to see him in sorrow,  
For all the treasure in the town of Troy,  
I pray God I may never have joy!"

126

"Now look then, if you, who are his love,  
Shall put all night his life in jeopardy  
For a thing of nought! Now, by that God above,  
Not only does this delay come from folly,  
But from malice, if I shall not lie.  
What, plainly, if you suffer him in distress,  
You neither do bounty nor gentleness!"

127

Then Cressida said, "Will you do one thing,  
And thereby will you stanch all his disease:  
Take this and bear him this blue ring.  
For there is nothing that would please him better,  
Except myself, nor more appease his heart;  
And tell my dear heart that his sorrow  
Is groundless, that shall be seen tomorrow."

128

"A ring?" he said, "Ay, the hazel woods be shaken!  
Yes, niece mine, that ring must have a stone  
That could make dead men alive again;  
And such a ring, I believe you have none.  
Discretion has gone out of your head;  
That I feel now," said he, "and that is pity;  
O time lost, well may you curse your sloth!"

129

"Know you not well that noble and high courage  
Does not grieve nor cease for a little thing?  
But if a fool were in a jealous rage,  
I would not esteem his sorrow a mite.  
But give him a few fitting words another day,  
When I might find him: yet this matter stands  
In a quite different kind."

130

"This man is so gentle and so tender of heart,  
That with his death he will avenge his sorrows;  
For trust well, however sorely he smart,  
He will to you speak no jealous words.  
And therefore, niece, before his heart break,  
So speak yourself to him of this matter;  
For with one word you may stir his heart."

131

"Now have I told what peril he is in,  
And his coming is unknown to every person;  
No, by God, there may be neither harm nor sin;  
I myself will be with you all this night.  
You know also how he is your own knight,  
And that, by right, you must place trust in him,  
And I am ready to fetch him when you list."

132

This accident was so pitiful to hear,  
And also so like the truth at first sight,  
And Troilus her knight to her so dear,  
His private coming, and the secure place,  
That, though she then showed him a favor,  
She considered all things as they stood.  
No wonder it is, since she did all for good.

133

Cressida answered, "As truly as God rest my soul,  
I am sorrowful for him! And, indeed, truly,  
I would gladly do the best that I can.  
If I had grace to do so.  
But whether you remain or go for him,  
I am, until God send me better counsel,  
At Dulcarnon, right at my wit's end."

134

Quoth Pandarus, "Yes, niece, will you hear?  
Dulcarnon is called the 'failing of wretches';  
It seems hard, for wretches will not learn  
By sheer sloth or other willful faults;  
This said by those who are not worth two farthings.  
But you are wise, and that which we have in hand  
Is neither hard nor skilful to withstand."

135

"Then, uncle," she said, "Do as you think best;  
But ere he comes I will first arise;  
And, for the love of God, since all my trust  
Is on you two, and you are both wise,  
So devise now in so discreet a wise  
That I may have honor and he pleasure;  
For I am here entirely in your governance."

136

"That is well said," he said, "my dear niece;  
There is good thrift in that wise gentle heart!  
But lie still, and take him right here;  
There is no need for him to go further;  
And each of you may ease the other's sorrow.  
For the love of God; and, Venus, I praise thee;  
For soon I hope we shall all be merry."

137

This Troilus full soon set himself on knees  
Full soberly, right by her bed's head,  
And in his best fashion greeted his lady;  
But, lord, how she suddenly grew red!  
No, though men should strike off her head,  
She could not bring forth one proper word  
So suddenly, for his sudden coming.

138

But Pandarus, who so well could feel  
In every thing, to play forthwith began,  
And said, "Niece, see how this lord can kneel!  
Now, for your truth, look on this gentle man!"  
And with that word he ran for a cushion,  
And said, "Kneel now, while it pleases you;  
There God bring your hearts soon to rest!"

139

I cannot say whether she bade him not rise,  
If sorrow drove the thought from her remembrance,  
Or else if she took it in the manner  
Of duty, for his observance;  
But well I find she did him this favor,  
That she kissed him, although she sighed sore,  
And bade him sit down without further word.

140

Quoth Pandarus, "Now will you well begin;  
Now set him to sit, good niece dear,  
Upon your bed's side there within,  
That each of you the better may hear the other."  
And with that word he drew him to the fire,  
And took a light, and set his countenance  
As if to look upon an old romance.

# BOOK III

### Middle English

Criseyde, that was Troilus lady right,  
And cleer stood on a ground of sikernesse,  
Although she, hir servaunt and hir knight  
Ne sholde of right non untrouthe in hir gesse.  
Yet nathelees, considered his distresse,  
And that love is in cause of swich folye,  
Thus to him spak she of his jelousye:

“Lo, herte myn, as wolde the excellence  
Of love, ayeins the which that no man may,  
Ne oghte eek goodly maken resistence;  
And eek bycause I felte wel and say  
Your grete trouthe, and servyse every day;  
And that your herte al myn was, sooth to seyne,  
This droof me for to rewe upon your peyne.

And your goodnesse have I founde alwey yit,  
Of whiche, my dere herte and al my knight,  
I thonke it yow, as fer as I have wit,  
Al can I nought as muche as it were right;  
And I, emforth my conninge and my might,  
Have and ay shal, how sore that me smerte,  
Ben to yow trewe and hool, with al myn herte;

And dredeles, that shal be founde at preve. —  
But, herte myn, what al this is to seyne  
Shal wel be told, so that ye noght yow greve,  
Though I to yow right on your-self compleyne.  
For ther-with mene I fynally the peyne,  
That halt your herte and myn in hevinesse,  
Fully to sleen, and every wrong redresse.

My goode, myn, not I for-why ne how  
That Jalousye, alas! that wikked wivere,  
Thus causelees is cropen in-to yow;  
The harm of which I wolde fayn delivere!  
Allas! that he, al hool, or of him slivere,  
Should have his reflut in so digne a place,  
Ther Jove him sone out of your herte arace.

But O, thou Jove, O auctor of nature,  
Is this an honour to thy deitee,  
That folk ungiltif suffren here injure,  
And who that giltif is, al quit goth he?  
O were it leful for to pleyne on thee,  
That undeserved suffrest jalousye,  
And that I wolde up-on thee pleyne and crye!

Eek al my wo is this, that folk now usen  
To seyn right thus, ‘Ye, jealousy is Love!’  
And wolde a busshel venim al excusen,  
For that one grein of love is on it shove!  
But that wot heighe god that sit above,  
If it be lyker love, or hate, or grame;  
And after that, it oghte bere his name.

But certeyn is, som maner jealousy  
Is excusable more than som, ywis;

As whan ther’s cause, or whan folk long repress  
Som harsh fantastic notion in hir minds,  
Which in expression no free outlet finds,  
And on itself it thus doth grow and feed;  
For such repression is a gentle deed.

And some so ful of furie is and despite  
That it sourmounteth his repressioun;  
But herte myn, ye be not in that plyt,  
That thanke I God, for whiche your passioun  
I wol not calle it but illusioun  
Of habundaunce of love and bisy cure,  
That dooth your herte this disese endure.

Of which I am right sory, but not wrooth;  
But, for my devoir and your hertes reste,  
Wher-so yow list, by ordal or by ooth,  
By sort, or in what wyse so yow leste,  
For love of God, lat preve it for the beste!  
And if that I be giltif, do me deye,  
Allas! what mighte I more doon or seye?”

With that a fewe brighte teres newe  
Out of hir eyen fille, and thus she seyde,  
“Now God, thou wost, in thought ne dede untrewe  
To Troilus was never yet Criseyde.”  
With that hir heed doun in the bed she leyde,  
And with the shete it wreigh, and syghed sore,  
And held hir pees; not a word spak she more.

Ther-with the sorwe so his herte shette,  
That from his eyen fil ther not a tere,  
And every spirit his vigour in-knettes  
So they astoned and oppressed were.  
The feling of his sorwes or of his fere  
Or of ought elles, fled was out of towne;  
And doun he fel al sodeynly a-swowne.

This was no litel sorwe for to se;  
But al was hust, and Pandare up as faste,  
“O nece, pees, or we be lost,” quod he,  
“Beth nought agast”; but certeyn, at the laste,  
For this or that, he into bedde him caste,  
And seyde, “O theef, is this a mannes herte?”  
And of he rente al to his bare sherte;

And seyde, “Nece, but ye helpe us now,  
Allas, your owne Troilus is lorn!”  
“Y-wis, so wolde I, and I wiste how,  
Ful fayn,” quod she; “alas! that I was born!”  
“Ye, nece, wol ye pullen out the thorn  
That stiketh in his herte?” quod Pandare;  
“Sey ‘al foryeve,’ and stint is all this fare!”

“Ye, that to me,” quod she, “ful lever were  
Than al the good the sonne aboute gooth”;  
And therwith-al she swoor him in his ere,  
“Y-wis, my dere herte, I am nought wrooth.  
Have here my trouthe and many another ooth;  
Now speek to me, for it am I, Criseyde!”

But al for nought; yet mighte he not abreyde.  
Therwith his pous and pawmes of his hondes  
They gan to frote, and wete his temples tweyne.  
And, to deliveren him from bittre bondes,  
She ofte him kiste; and, shortly for to seyne,  
Him to revoken she dide al hir peyne.  
And at the laste, he gan his breeth to drawe,  
And of his swough sone after that adawe,  
And gan bet minde and reson to him take.  
But wonder sore he was abayst, ywis.  
And with a syk, whan he gan bet a-wake,  
He seyde, “O mercy, God, what thing is this?”  
“Why do ye with yourselves thus amis?”  
Quod tho Criseyde, “Is this a mannes game?  
What, Troilus! wol ye do thus, for shame?”

And therwith-al hir arm over him she leyde,  
And al foryaf, and ofte tyme him keste.  
He thonked hir, and to hir spak, and seyde  
As fil to purpos for his herte reste.  
And she to that answerde him as hir leste;  
And with hir goodly wordes him disporte  
She gan, and ofte his sorwes to comforte.

Quod Pandarus, “for ought I can espyen,  
This light nor I ne serven here of nought;  
Light is not good for syke folkes’ yen.  
But for the love of God, sin ye be brought  
In thus good plyt, lat now non hevy thought  
Ben hanginge in the hertes of yow tweye”;  
And bar the candel to the chimeneye.

Sone after this, though it no ned were,  
Whan she swich othes as hir list devyse  
Hadde of him take, hir thoughte tho no fere,  
Ne cause eek non, to bidde him thennes ryse.  
Yet lesse thing than othes may suffyse  
In many a cas; for every wight, I gesse,  
That loveth wel meneth but gentilesse.

But in effect she wolde wite anoon  
Of what man, and eek where, and also why  
He jelous was, sin ther was cause noon;  
And eek the signe, that he took it by,  
She bad him that to telle hir bisily,  
Or elles, certeyn, she bar him on honde,  
That this was doon of malis, hir to fonde.

With-outen more, shortly for to seyne,  
He moste obeye unto his lady heste;  
And for the lasse harm, he moste feyne.  
He seyde hir, whan she was at swich a feste  
She mighte on him han loked at the leste;  
Not I not what, al dere y-nough a risshe,  
As he that nedes moste a cause fisshe.

And she answerde, “Swete, al were it so,  
What harm was that, sin I non yvel mene?  
For, by that God that boughte us bothe two,  
In alle thinge is myn entente clene.  
Swich arguments ne been not worth a bene;  
Wol ye the childish jalous contrefete?  
Now were it worthy that ye were y-bete.”

Tho Troilus gan sorwfully to syke,  
Lest she be wrooth, him thoughte his herte deyde;  
And seyde, “Alas! upon my sorwes syke  
Have mercy, swete herte myn, Criseyde!  
And if that, in tho wordes that I seyde,  
Be any wrong, I wol no more trespace;  
Do what yow list, I am al in your grace.”

And she answerde, “Of gilt misericorde!  
That is to seyn, that I foryeve al this;  
And ever-more on this night yow recorde.  
And beth wel war ye do no more amis.”  
“Nay, dere herte myn,” quod he, “ywIS.”  
“And now,” quod she, “that I have do yow smerte,  
Foryeve it me, myn owene swete herte.”

This Troilus, with blisse of that suprysed,  
Put al in goddes hond, as he that mente  
No-thing but wel; and, sodeynly avysed,  
He hir in armes faste to him hente.  
And Pandarus, with a ful good entente,  
Leyde him to slepe, and seyde, “If ye ben wyse,  
Swowneth not now, lest more folk aryse.”

What mighte or may the sely larke seye,  
Whan that the sparhauk hath it in his foot?

I can no more, but of thise ilke tweye  
To whom this tale sucre be or soot.  
Though that I tarie a yeer, somtyme I moot,  
After myn auctor, tellen hir gladnesse,  
As wel as I have told hir hevinesse.

Criseyde, which that felte hir thus y-take,  
Right as writen clerkes in hir bokes olde,  
Right as an aspes leef she gan to quake,  
Whan she him felte hir in his armes folde.  
But Troilus, al hool of cares colde,  
Gan thanken tho the blisful goddes sevene;  
Thus sondry peynes bringen folk to hevene.

This Troilus in armes gan hir streyne,  
And seyde, “O swete, as ever mote I goon.  
Now be ye caught, now is ther but we tweyne;  
Now yeldeth yow, for other boot is noon.”  
To that Criseyde answerde thus anoon,  
“Ne hadde I er now, my swete herte dere,  
Ben yolde, ywis, I were now not here!”

O! sooth is seyd, that heled for to be  
As of a fevre or othere greet syknesse,  
Men moste drinke, as men may often see,  
Ful bittre drink; and for to han gladnesse  
Men drinken often peyne and greet distresse;  
I mene it here, as for this aventure,  
That thourgh a peyne hath founden al his cure.

And now swetnesse semeth more swete,  
That bitternesse assayed was biforn;  
For out of wo in blisse now they flete.  
Non swich they felten, sith they were born;  
Now is this bet, than bothe two be lorn!  
For love of God, take every womman hede  
To werken thus, if it comth to the nede.

Criseyde, al quit from every drede and tene,  
As she that juste cause hadde him to triste,  
Made him swich feste, it joye was to sene,  
Whan she his trouthe and clene entente wiste.  
And as about a tree, with many a twiste,  
Bitrent and wryth the sote wode-binde,  
Gan eche of hem in armes other winde.

And as the newe abaysshed nightingale,  
That stinteth first whan she biginneth singe,  
Whan that she hereth any herde tale,  
Or in the hegges any wight steringe,  
And after siker dooth hir vois out-ringe;  
Right so Criseyde, whan hir drede stente,  
Opened hir herte, and tolde him hir entente.

And right as he that seeth his deeth y-shapen,  
And deye moot, in ought that he may gesse,  
And sodeynly rescous doth him escapen,  
And from his deeth is brought in sikemesse,  
For al this world, in swich present gladnesse  
Was Troilus, and hath his lady swete;  
With worse hap God lat us never mete!

Her armes smale, hir streyghte bak and softe,  
Hir sydes longe, fleshy, smothe, and whyte  
He gan to stroke, and good thrift bad ful ofte  
Hir snowish throte, hir brestes rounde and lyte;  
Thus in this hevene he gan him to delyte,  
And ther-with-al a thousand tyme hir kiste;  
That, what to done, for joye unnethe he wiste.

Than seyde he thus, “O, Love, O, Charite,  
Thy moder eek, Citherea the swete,  
After thy-self next heried be she,  
Venus men I, the wel-willy planete;  
And next that, Hymen, I thee grete;  
For never man was to yow goddes holde  
As I, which ye han brought fro cares colde.

Benigne Love, thou holy bond of thinges,  
Who-so wol grace, and list thee nought honouren,  
Lo, his desyr wol flee with-outen winges.  
For, noldestow of bountee hem socouren  
That serven best and most alwey labouren,  
Yet were al lost, that dar I wel seyn, certes,  
But-if thy grace passed our desertes.

And for thou me, that coude leest deserve  
Of hem that nombred been unto thy grace,  
Hast holpen, ther I lykly was to sterve.

### Modern English (Translation)

Cressida, who was truly Troilus’s lady,  
And stood clear on a ground of security,  
Although she thought that her servant and her knight  
Should in no way be considered unfaithful to her.  
Yet nonetheless, considering his distress,  
And knowing that love is often the cause of such folly,  
Thus she spoke to him about his jealousy:

“My heart, if only the excellence of love —  
Against which no man can or ought to resist —  
Were such that, because I have seen and known  
Your great fidelity and daily service,  
And that your heart has been entirely mine, indeed,  
I am moved to pity for your pain.

And your goodness I have found always to remain,  
For which, my dear heart and my entire knight,  
I thank you as far as my wit allows;  
I cannot fully give you as much as were your due,  
Yet, with my knowledge and my strength,  
Have, and always shall, however it may grieve me,  
Be to you true and whole with all my heart;

And doubtless, that shall be proved. —  
But, my heart, whatever this is to say  
Shall be told you well, so that you do not take offense,  
Although I seem to complain directly to you.  
For by this I mean finally the pain  
That holds your heart and mine in heaviness,  
To slay it utterly, and to right every wrong.

My good one, I do not know why or how  
That jealousy, alas! that wicked serpent,  
Should thus, without cause, creep into you;  
The harm of which I would gladly deliver you!  
Alas! that he, wholly himself or any part of him,  
Should have such refuge in so worthy a place,  
That Jove might quickly pluck him out of your heart.

But O, thou Jove, O author of nature,  
Is this an honor to thy deity,  
That innocent folk here should suffer wrong,  
And the guilty one go free altogether?  
O that it were lawful to complain of thee,  
I would accuse thy mighty name,  
For undeserved jealousy I bear the blame.

Also my sorrow is that folk now use  
To say straight out, ‘Yes, jealousy is Love!’  
And they would excuse a bushel of venom,  
If they can thrust in a grain of love with it!  
But God on high who sits above knows best  
Whether it is more like love or like hate or wrath;  
And by that it ought to be named.

But certainly some kinds of jealousy  
Are more excusable than others, indeed;

As when there is cause, or when men long repress  
Some harsh fantastic notion in their minds,  
Which finds no free outlet in expression,  
And thus feeds and grows upon itself;  
For such repression is a gentle deed.

And some are full of fury and despite,  
So that they overcome all restraint;  
But, my heart, you are not in that plight,  
For which I thank God, and therefore your passion  
I will not call anything but illusion  
From abundance of love and busy care,  
That makes your heart endure this disease.

Of which I am truly sorry, but not angry;  
But, for my duty and for the rest of your heart,  
Wherever you please, by ordeal or by oath,  
By lot, or in whatever way you think best,  
For God’s love, let it be proven for the best!  
And if I be guilty, put me to death!  
Alas! what more might I do or say?”

With that a few bright tears newly  
Fell from her eyes, and thus she said,  
“Now God, thou knowest, in thought nor deed untrue  
Was Cressida to Troilus never yet.”  
With that her head she laid down in the bed,  
And with the sheet wrapped it, and sighed sore,  
And held her peace; not another word she spoke.

Though sorrow so shut his heart within,  
From his eyes there fell not a single tear,  
And every spirit, his vigor bound and knotted,  
So that they were astounded and oppressed.  
The sense of his sorrows or of his fear  
Or anything else fled was out of the town;  
And down he fell all suddenly into a swoon.

This was no little sorrow to see;  
But all was hushed, and Pandarus up as fast,  
“Hush, niece, peace, or we are lost,” said he,  
“Be not afraid”; but certain, at the last,  
For this or that, he cast him into the bed,  
And said, “O thief, is this a man’s heart?”  
And he tore off all to his bare shirt;

And said, “Niece, unless you help us now,  
Alas, your own Troilus is lost!”  
“Truly, I would gladly, if I knew how,”  
Said she; “alas! that I was born!”  
“Yes, niece, will you pull out the thorn  
That sticks in his heart?” said Pandarus;  
“Say ‘All is forgiven,’ and the matter is ended!”

“That were to me,” she said, “far dearer  
Than all the goods the circling sun goes round.”  
And thereupon she swore him in his ear,  
“By all the oaths by which I can be bound,  
I am not angry,”— yet he made no sound.  
“It is Cressida, O speak, my precious heart!”  
But from his trance she could not awaken him.

His wrists and palms then they began to rub,  
And they wetted both his temples with water,  
To free him from his bitter bonds,  
And often she kissed him; and, in short,  
All her pains she took to call him back.  
And at the last he began to draw breath,  
And of his swoon soon after that came awake,  
And began to take better mind and reason.  
But he was sorely abashed, truly.  
And with a sigh, when he was better awake,  
He said, “O mercy, God, what thing is this?”  
“Why do you behave thus to yourself?”  
Said then Cressida, “Is this a man’s game?  
What, Troilus! will you act thus, for shame?”

And with that her arm she laid over him,  
And forgave him, and kissed him oftentimes.  
He thanked her, and to her spoke and said  
Such things as served to purpose for his heart’s rest.  
And she answered him as pleased her;  
And with her goodly words she comforted him,  
And oft set his sorrows to rest.

Said Pandarus, “For all that I can espy,  
This light nor I serve not here for naught;  
Light is not good for sick folk’s eyes.  
But for the love of God, since you are brought  
Into such good plight, let now no heavy thought  
Be hanging in the hearts of you two”;  
And he carried the candle to the chimney-side.

Soon after this, though it were not needful,  
When she had taken such oaths as she pleased of him,  
She thought then no fear, nor cause too, to bid him rise.  
Yet less than oaths may suffice in many a case;  
For every person, I suppose, who loves well  
Means but gentleness.

But in effect she would know at once  
Of what man, and also where, and why  
He was jealous, since there was no cause;  
And also the sign by which he took it,  
She bade him tell that to her busily,  
Or else, certainly, she swore in his hand,  
That this was done from malice, to spy on her.

Without more, shortly to say,  
He must obey unto his lady’s command;  
And for the less harm, he must feign.  
He said to her, when she was at such a feast  
She might have looked on him at least;  
Not I know what, a dear enough jest,  
As he that needs must find a cause.

And she answered, “Sweetheart, though it were so,  
What harm was that, since I meant no evil?  
For, by that God who bought us both two,  
In all things is my intent pure.  
Such arguments are not worth a bean;  
Will you the childish jealous counterfeit?  
Now it were worthy that you were beaten.”

Then Troilus began sorrowfully to sigh,  
Lest she be wroth, him thought his heart died;  
And said, “Alas! upon my sorrows sigh,  
Have mercy, sweet heart of mine, Cressida!  
And if in those words that I said  
Be any wrong, I will no more trespass;  
Do what you list, I am all in your grace.”

And she answered, “Of kindly mercy!  
That is to say, I forgive all this;  
And evermore this night I record you.  
And be well wary you do no more amiss.”  
“Nay, dear heart of mine,” said he, “ywise.”  
“And now,” said she, “that I have done you harm,  
Forgive it me, my own sweet heart.”

This Troilus, with the bliss of that surprised,  
Put all in God’s hand, as he who meant  
Nothing but well; and suddenly advised,  
He took her in his arms fast to him.  
And Pandarus, with a full good intent,  
Laid him to sleep, and said, “If you be wise,  
Swoon not now, lest more folk arise.”

What might or may the poor lark say,  
When the sparrow-hawk hath it in his foot?

I can no more, but of these same two —  
To whom this tale sugar be or soot.  
Though I should tarry a year, sometime I must,  
After my author, tell their gladness,  
As well as I have told their heaviness.

Cressida, who felt herself thus taken,  
As clerks write in their old books,  
Like an aspen leaf she began to quake,  
When she felt him fold her in his arms.  
But Troilus, all whole of care and cold,  
Gave thanks then to the blissful gods seven;  
Thus sundry pains bring folk to heaven.

Troilus in arms began to press her,  
And said, “O sweet, as ever I may go.  
Now you are caught, now are there but we twain;  
Now yield yourself, for other boot is none.”  
To that Cressida answered thus anon,  
“Had I not yielded earlier, my sweet heart dear,  
Truly, I were now not here!”

O! true it is, that to be healed  
As from a fever or other great sickness,  
One must drink, as one may often see,  
A very bitter draught; and to have gladness  
Men oft drink pain and great distress;  
I mean it here, as for this adventure,  
That through a pain he found all his cure.

And now sweetness seems the more sweet,  
That bitterness had been tried before;  
For out of woe into bliss now they flee.  
None such they felt since they were born;  
Now is this better than both being lost!  
For God’s love, every woman take heed  
To work thus, if it come to the need.

Cressida, all quit from every dread and grief,  
As she who had just cause to trust him,  
Made him such feast, it was joy to see,  
When she knew his troth and clean intent.  
And like the woodbine, with many a twist,  
Wound and entwined the sweet wood-bind,  
So each of them their arms about the other wind.

And as the new, abashed nightingale,  
That stints first when she begins to sing,  
When she hears any herding tale,  
Or in the hedges any creature stirring,  
And after sure lets her voice ring forth;  
Right so Cressida, when her dread ceased,  
Opened her heart, and told him her intent.

And just as he who sees his death fashioned,  
And must die, in anything that he may guess,  
And suddenly rescue causes him to escape,  
And from his death is brought to sickness,  
For all this world, in such present gladness  
Was Troilus, and has his lady sweet;  
With worse hap God let us never meet!

Her arms small, her straight back and soft,  
Her long sides, fleshy, smooth, and white,  
He began to stroke, and often well regarded  
Her snowy throat, her round and modest breasts;  
Thus in this heaven he began to delight,  
And withal a thousand times he kissed her;  
That, what to do, for joy hardly he knew.

Then said he thus, “O Love, O Charity,  
Thy mother also, Cytherea the sweet,  
After thyself next be praised she,  
Venus I mean, the kindly planet;  
And next that, Hymen, I greet thee;  
For never man was held to you gods  
As I, whom ye have brought from cold cares.

Benign Love, thou holy bond of things,  
Whoever wills grace, and lists not to honor thee,  
Lo, his desire will flee without wings.  
For, wouldst thou not of bounty succor those  
That serve best and most always labor,  
Yet were all lost, that dare I well say, certes,  
Unless thy grace passed our deserts.

And for thou me, that could least deserve  
Of those that are numbered unto thy grace,  
Hast helped, where I likely was to perish.

## BOOK III

### Middle English

And therwith-al Criseyde anoon he kiste,  
Of which, certein, she felte no disese.

And thus seyde he, “Now wolde God I wiste,  
Myn herte swete, how I yow mighte plese!  
What man,” quod he, “was ever thus at ese  
As I, on which the faireste and the beste  
That ever I say, deyneth hir herte reste.

Here may men seen that mercy passeth right;  
The experience of that is felt in me.  
That am unworthy to so swete a wight.  
But herte myn, of your benignitee  
So thenketh, though that I unworthy be,  
Yet mot I nede amenden in som wise,  
Right thourgh the vertu of your heighe servise.

And for the love of God, my lady dere,  
Sin God hath wrought me for I shal yow serve,  
As thus I mene, that ye wol be my stere,  
To do me live, if that yow liste, or sterve.  
So techeth me how that I may deserve  
Your thank, so that I, thurgh myn ignoraunce,  
Ne do no-thing that yow be displesaunce.

For certes, fresh wommanliche wif,  
This dar I seye, that trouthe and diligence  
That shal ye finden in me al my lyf,  
Ne I wol not, certein, breken your defence;  
And if I do, present or in absence,  
For love of God, lat slee me with the dede,  
If that it lyke unto your womanhede.”

“Y-wis,” quod she, “myn owne hertes list,  
My ground of ese, and al myn herte dere,  
Graunt mercy, for on that is al my trist;  
But late us falle awey fro this matere;  
For it suffyseth, this that seyd is here.  
And at o word, with-outen repentaunce,  
Wel-come, my knight, my pees, my suffisaunce!”

Of hir delyt, or joyes oon the leste  
Were impossible to my wit to seye;  
But juggeth, ye that han ben at the feste  
Of swich gladnesse, if that hem liste pley!  
I can no more, but thus thise ilke tweye  
That night, betwixen dreed and sikemesse,  
Felten in love the grete worthinesse.

O blisful night, of hem so longe y-sought,  
How blithe unto hem bothe two thou were!  
Why ne hadde I swich on with my soule y-bought,  
Ye, or the leeste joye that was there?  
A-wey, thou foule daunger and thou fere,  
And lat hem in this hevene blisse dwelle,  
That is so heigh, that al ne can I telle!

But sooth is, though I can not tellen al,  
As can myn auctor, of his excellence.  
Yet have I seyd, and, God to-forn, I shal  
In every thing al hoolly his sentence.  
And if that I, at loves reverence,  
Have any word in eched for the beste,  
Doth therwith-al right as your-selven leste.

For myne wordes, here and every part,  
I speke hem alle under correccioun  
Of yow, that feling han in loves art.  
And putte it al in your discrecioun  
To encrese or maken diminucioun  
Of my langage, and that I yow biseche;  
But now to purpos of my rather speche.

Thise ilke two, that ben in armes laft,  
So looth to hem a-sonder goon it were,  
That ech from other wende been biraft,  
Or elles, lo, this was hir moste fere,  
That al this thing but nyce dremes were;  
For which ful ofte ech of hem seyde, “O swete,  
Clippe ich yow thus, or elles I it mete?”

And, lord! so he gan goodly on hir se,  
That never his look ne bleynte from hir face,  
And seyde, “O dere herte, may it be  
That it be sooth, that ye ben in this place?”  
“Ye, herte myn, God thank I of his grace!”  
Quod tho Criseyde, and therwith-al him kiste.  
That where his spirit was, for joye he niste.

This Troilus ful ofte hir eyen two  
Gan for to kisse, and seyde, “O eyen clere,  
Ye be the cause of alle that me is smerte;  
Ye double wepnes of my lady dere!  
Though mercy seeme to be writen here,  
The text, forsooth, is ful hard for me to finde:  
How coude ye with-outen bond me binde?”

Therwith he gan hir faste in armes take,  
And wel an hundred tymes gan he syke;  
Nought swich sorrowful sykes as men make  
For wo, or when that folk ben sicke;  
But esy sykes, swiche as been to lyke,  
That shewed his affeccioun with-inne;  
Of swiche sykes coude he nought bilinne.

Sone after this they speke of sondry thinges,  
As fil to purpos of this aventure,  
And pleyinge entrechaungeden hir ringes,  
Of which I can nought tellen no scripture;  
But wel I woot a broche, gold and asure,  
In whiche a ruby set was lyk an herte,  
Criseyde him yaf, and stak it on his sherte.

Lord, trowe ye, a covetous, a wrecche,  
That blameth love and holt of it despit,  
That, of tho pens that he can moke and keche,  
Was ever yet y-yeve him swich delit,  
As is in love, in o poynt, in som plyt?  
Nay, doutelees, for also God me save,  
So parfit joye may no nigard have!

They wol seye “Yis,” but lord! so that they lye,  
Tho bisy wrecches, ful of wo and drede!  
They callen love a woodnesse or folye.  
But it shal falle hem as I shal yow rede;  
They shul forgo the white and eke the rede,  
And live in wo, ther God yeve hem mischaunce,  
And every lover in his trouthe avaunce!

As wolde God, tho wrecches that despyse  
Servyse of love, hadde eres al-so longe  
As hadde Myda, ful of covetise,  
And ther-to dronken hadde as hoot and stronge  
As Crassus dide for his affectis wronge,  
To techen hem that they ben in the vyce,  
And lovers nought, al-though they holde hem nyce!

Thise like two, of whom that I yow seye,  
Whan that hir hertes wel assured were,  
Tho gonne they to speken and to pleyen,  
And eek rehercen how, and whanne, and where.  
They knewe hem first, and every wo and fere  
That passed was; but al swich hevinesse,  
I thanke it God, was turned to gladnesse.

And evermo, whan that hem fel to speke  
Of any thing of swich a tyme agoon,  
With kissing al that tale sholde breke,  
And fallen in a newe joye anoon,  
And diden al hir might, sin they were oon,  
For to recoveren blisse and been at ese,  
And passed wo with joye counterpeyse.

Reson wil not that I speke of sleep;  
For it accordeth nought to my matere;  
God wot, they toke of that ful litel keep,  
But lest this night, that was to hem so dere,  
Ne sholde in veyn escape in no manere,  
It was biset in joye and bisinesse  
Of al that souneth in-to gentilnesse.

But whan the cok, comune astrologer,  
Gan on his brest to bete, and after crowe,  
And Lucifer, the dayes messager,  
Gan for to rise, and out hir bemes throwe;  
And estward roos, to him that coude it knowe,  
Fortuna maiour, than anoon Criseyde,  
With herte sore, to Troilus thus seyde:—

“Myn hertes lyf, my trist and my plesaunce,  
That I was born, alais! what me is wo,  
That day of us mot make desseveraunce!  
For tyme it is to rise, and hennes go.  
Or elles I am lost for evermo!  
O night, alais! why niltow over us hove,  
As longe as whan Almena lay by Jove?

O blake night, as folk in bokes rede,  
That shapen art by God this world to hyde  
At certein tymes with thy derke wede,  
That under that men might in reste abyde,  
We oghte bestes pleyne, and folk thee chyde,  
That there-as day with labour wol be us breste,  
That thou thus fleest, and deynest us nought reste!

Thou dost, alais! to shortly thyn office,  
Thou rakel night, ther God, makere of kinde,  
Thee, for thyn hast and thyn unkind office,  
So faste ay to our hemisphere binde,  
That never-more under the ground thou winde!  
For now, for thou so hyest out of Troye,  
Have I forgon thus hastily my joye!”

This Troilus, that with tho wordes felte,  
As thoughte him tho, for piteous distresse,  
The blody teres from his herte melte,  
As he that never yet swich hevinesse  
Assayed hadde, out of so grete gladnesse,  
Gan therwith-al Criseyde his lady dere  
In armes streyne, and seyde in this manere:—

“O cruel day, accusour of the joye  
That night and love han stole and faste ywryen,  
Acursed be thy coming in-to Troye,  
For every bore hath oon of thy bright yen!  
Envious day, what list thee so to spyen?  
What hastow lost, why sekestow this place,  
Ther God thy light so quenche, for his grace?

Allas! what han thise loveres thee agilt,  
Dispitous day? thyn be the pyne of helle!  
For many a lovere hastow shent, and wilt;  
Thy pouring in wol no-where lete hem dwelle.  
What proferestow thy light here for to selle?  
Go selle it hem that smale sceles graven;  
We wol thee nought, us nedeth no day haven.”

And eke the sonne Titan gan he chyde,  
And seyde, “O fool, wel may men thee despise,  
That hast the Dawing al night by thy syde,  
And suffrest hir so sone up fro thee ryse,  
For to disenesen lovers in this wise;  
What! holde thy bed ther, thou, and eek thy morwe!  
I biddeth God, so yeve yow bothe sorwe!”

Therwith ful sore he sighte, and thus he seyde,  
“My lady right, and of my wele or wo  
The welle and rote, O goodly myn, Criseyde,  
And shal I rise, alais! and shal I go?  
Now fele I that myn herte moot a-two!  
For how sholde I my lyf an houre save,  
Sin that with yow is al the lyf I have?

What shal I doon, for certes, I not how,  
Ne whanne, alais! I shal the tyme see  
That in this pleyt I may be eft with yow;  
And of my lyf, God woot how that shal be,  
Sin that desyr right now so byteth me,  
That I am deed anoon, but I retourne.  
How sholde I longe, alais! fro you sojourn?

But natheles, myn owene lady bright,  
Yit were it so that I wiste outrely,  
That I, your humble servaunt and your knight,  
Were in your herte set so fermely  
As ye in myn, the which thing, trewely,  
Me lever were than these worldes tweine,  
Yet sholde I bet endure al my peyne.

To that Criseyde answerde right anoon,  
And with a syk she seyde, “O herte dere,  
The game, ywis, so ferforth now is goon,  
That first shal Phebus falle fro his spere,  
And every eagle been the doves fere,  
And every roche out of his place sterte,  
Er Troilus out of Criseydes herte!

Ye be so depe in with myn herte grave,  
That, though I wolde it turne out of my thought,  
As wisely verray God my soule save,  
To dyen in the peine, I coude nought!  
And for the love of God that us hath wrought,  
Lat in your brain non other fantasye  
So crepe, that it cause me to dye!

And that ye me wolde han as faste in minde  
As I have yow, that wolde I yow biseche;  
And, if I wiste soothly that to finde,  
God mighte not a poynt my joyes eche!  
But, herte myn, withouten more speche,  
Beth to me trewe, or elles were it routhe;  
For I am thyn, by God and by my trouthe!

Beth glad forthy, and live in sikemesse;  
Thus seyde I never er this, ne shal to mo;  
And if to yow it were a greet gladnesse  
To turne ayein, soone after that ye go,  
As fayn wolde I as ye, it were so,  
As wysly God myn herte bringe at reste!”  
And him in armes took, and ofte keste.

Agains his wil, sin it moot nedes be.  
This Troilus up roos, and faste him cledde,  
And in his armes took his lady free  
An hundred tyme, and on his wey him spedde,  
And with swich wordes as his herte bledde  
He seyde, “Farewel, my dere herte swete,  
Ther God us graunte sounde and sone to mete!”

To which no word for sorwe she answerde;  
So sore gan his parting hir destreyne;  
And Troilus unto his palays ferde,  
As woo bigon as she was, sooth to seyne;  
So hard him wrong of sharp desyr the peyne  
For to ben eft there he was in plesaunce,  
That it may never out of his remembraunce.

Retorned to his royal palais, sone  
He softe into his bed gan for to slinke,  
To slepe longe, as he was wont to doon.  
But al for nought; he may wel ligge and winke,  
But sleep ne may ther in his herte sinke;  
Thenkinge how she, for whom desyr him brende,  
A thousandfold was worth more than he wende.

And in his thought gan up and doun to winde  
Hir wordes alle, and every contenaunce,  
And fermely impressen in his minde  
The leste poynt that to him was plesaunce;  
And verrayliche, of thilke remembraunce,  
Desyr al newe him brende, and lust to brede  
Gan more than erst, and yet took he non hede.

Criseyde also, right in the same wise,  
Of Troilus gan in hir herte shette  
His worthinesse, his lust, his dedes wyse,  
His gentilesse, and how she with him mette,  
Thonkinge love he so wel hir bisette;  
Desyring eft to have hir herte dere  
In swich a plyt, she dorste make him chere.

Pandare, a-morwe which that comen was  
Unto his nece, and gan hir fayre grete,  
Seyde, “Al this night so reyned it, alais!  
That al my drede is that ye, nece swete,  
Han litel layser had to slepe and mete;  
Al night,” quod he, “hath reyn so do me wake,  
That som of us, I trowe, hir hedes ake.”

And ner he com, and seyde, “How stant it now  
This mery morwe, nece, how kan ye fare?”  
Criseyde answerde, “Never the bet for yow.  
Fox that ye been, God yeve your herte care!  
God helpe me so, ye caused al this fare.  
Trow I,” quod she, “for alle your wordes whyte;  
O! who-so seeth yow knoweth yow ful lyte!”

Criseyde strove her blushing face to hide  
Behind the bed, and grew for shame al red;  
But Pandare underneath the bedclothes pried,  
“Dere Criseyde, if I must dye,” he seyde,  
“Have here a swerd and smite off my poore hede!”  
He thrust his arm beneath her nek to twiste,  
The coveres of, and eft his nece he kiste.

No more of this: such mirth as hem befell,  
If God forgaf his dethe, she sholde nat let  
Hir uncle harmless be; and thus they whilede  
The time in freendly blis and muche quiete,  
Til at the last she to her house wente,  
And left her uncle at his ease ful fayn.

Pandare, that by Troilus was sent a-rowe,  
Came to his lord; and after greet greeting,  
Upon his bed he sat him doun to rove,  
And Troilus, that lay til that same thing,  
Namely the joy which Pandare did bring,  
Fell on his knees and thanked him ful ofte,  
And blessed him that his heviness he softe.

“O friend,” quod he, “of friends the very beste  
That ever was or ever was herd tell,  
Thou hast in hevene brought my soule to reste  
From Phlegethon, the fiery flood of hell;  
A thousand tyme a day if I wol sell  
My-self to serve and honour only thee,  
It wol nat be ymuch; thy thank is me.”

“The sun, that moveth in his course upright,  
Saw never yet, this dare I wel avowe,  
A fairer than my lady dere and bright;  
And to my deeth I shal be bounden now  
To her; the thank for al this I allow  
To Love, and eke to thee, my Pandare dere,  
For thy wise labour which me brought out of fere.”

### Modern English

“And me bistowed in so high a place  
That thilke boundes may no blissé pace,  
I can no more, but laude and reverence  
Be to thy bounte and thy excellence!”

And therewithal Criseyde anon he kist,  
Of which, certain, she felt no disese.

And thus said he, “Now would God I knew,  
My heart’s sweet, how I might please you!  
What man,” said he, “was ever thus at ease  
As I, on whom the fairest and the best  
That ever I saw deigns her heart to rest?

Here may men see that mercy passes right;  
The experience of that is felt in me,  
That am unworthy to so sweet a wight.  
But heart of mine, of your benignity  
So thinketh, though that I unworthy be,  
Yet must I needs amend in some wise,  
Right through the virtue of your high service.

And for the love of God, my lady dear,  
Since God hath wrought me that I shall you serve,  
As thus I mean, that ye will be my steer;  
To do me live, if that you list, or sterve.  
So teach me how that I may deserve  
Your thanks, so that I, through my ignorance,  
Do nothing that you be displeased.

For certes, fresh womanlike wife,  
This dare I say, that truth and diligence  
That shall ye find in me all my life;  
Nor will I not, certain, break your defence;  
And if I do, present or in absence,  
For love of God, let slay me with the dead,  
If that it please unto your womanhead.”

“Y-wis,” said she, “my own heart’s delight,  
My ground of ease, and all my heart’s dear,  
Grant mercy, for on that I build my trust;  
But let us fall away from this matter;  
For it sufficeth, this that here is said.  
And at one word, without repentance,  
Welcome, my knight, my peace, my sufficiency!”

Of her delight or joys, one least of them,  
Were impossible for my wit to tell;  
But judge, ye that have been at such a feast  
Of such gladness, if that it please you play!  
I can no more, but thus these same twain  
That night, betwixt dread and sickness,  
Felt in love the great worthiness.

O blissful night, of them so long sought,  
How blithe unto them both you were!  
Why had I such a one with my soul bought,  
Yea, or the least joy that was there?  
Away, thou foul danger and thou fear,  
And let them in this heaven-bliss dwell,  
That is so high, that all I cannot tell!

But truth is, though I cannot tell all,  
As can my author, of his excellence.  
Yet have I said, and, God before, I shall  
In everything wholly his sentence.  
And if I, at love’s reverence,  
Have any word that errs toward the best,  
Do therewithal right as yourselves think best.

For all my words, here and in every part,  
I speak them all under correction  
Of you that have feeling in love’s art.  
And put it all in your discretion  
To increase or make diminution  
Of my language, and that I you beseech;  
But now to purpose of my rather speech.

These same two, that lie yet in arms,  
So loath were they to part, it seemed,  
That each from other feared to be bereft,  
Or else—lo—this was their greatest fear,  
That all this thing were but nice dreams;  
For which full often each of them said, “O sweet,  
Cling I to you thus, or else I taste it?”

And, Lord! so he began to gaze upon her,  
That never his look averted from her face,  
And said, “O dear heart, may it be true  
That it is sooth that you are in this place?”  
“Yes, heart of mine, God thank I of his grace!”  
Quoth then Criseyde, and therewithal him kissed.  
Where was his spirit, for joy he knew not.

This Troilus full often her two eyes  
Began to kiss, and said, “O eyes so clear,  
Ye be the cause of all that me is smart;  
Ye double weapons of my lady dear!  
Though mercy seem to be writ here,  
The text, forsooth, is full hard for me to find:  
How could you without bonds me bind?”

Therewith he took her fast into his arms,  
And well an hundred times he sighed;  
Not such sorrowful sighs as men make  
For woe, or when folk are sick;  
But easy sighs, such as please,  
That showed his affection within;  
Of such sighs could he not refrain.

Soon after this they spoke of sundry things,  
As fell to purpose of this adventure,  
And playing exchanged their rings,  
Of which I can not tell a scripture;  
But well I wot a brooch, gold and azure,  
In which a ruby set was like a heart,  
Criseyde him gave, and stuck it on his shirt.

Lord! believe you, a covetous wretch,  
That blames love and holds it in despite,  
That, of those pens that he can mock and catch,  
Was ever yet given such delight,  
As is in love, in one point, in some play?  
Nay, doubtless, so help me God,  
So perfect joy may no niggard have!

They will say “Yes,” but, Lord! so that they lie,  
Those busy wretches, full of woe and dread!  
They call love madness or folly.  
But it shall befall them as I shall you read:  
They shall forgo the white and also the red,  
And live in woe—there God give them mischance—  
And every lover in his truth advance!

As would God those wretches that despise  
Service of love had ears as long  
As had Midas, full of covetousness;  
And thereto drunken had as hot and strong  
As Crassus drank for his wrongful acts,  
To teach them that they are in the vice,  
And not lovers, although they hold them nice!

These like two, of whom that I you tell,  
When that their hearts well assured were,  
Then began they to speak and also to play,  
And eke rehearse how, and when, and where.  
They knew each other first, and every woe and fear  
That passed was; but all such heaviness,  
I thank God, was turned to gladness.

And evermore, when it befell them to speak  
Of anything of such a time ago,  
With kissing all that tale they would break,  
And fall into a new joy anon,  
And did all their might, since they were one,  
To recover bliss and be at ease,  
And past woe with joy to counterpoise.

You’ll scarce expect that I should speak of sleep;  
The topic seems, indeed, not pertinent.  
God wot, they took but little keeping of that,  
But lest this night, that was to them so dear,  
Should in vain escape in no manner,  
It was busied in joy and busyness  
Of all that soundeth into gentleness.

But when the cock, common astrologer,  
Gan on his breast to beat, and after crow,  
And Lucifer, the day’s messenger,  
Began to rise and forth his beams to throw;  
And eastward rose, to him that could it know,  
Fortuna maiour—then anon Criseyde,  
With heart sore, to Troilus thus said:—

“My heart’s life, my trust and my pleasure,  
That I was born, alas! what me is woe,  
That day of us must make disseverance!  
For time it is to rise, and hence go.  
Or else I am lost for evermore!  
O night, alas! why wilt thou not hover o’er us,  
As long as when Alcmena lay by Jove?

O black night, as folk in books read,  
That art made by God this world to hide  
At certain times with thy dark weed,  
That under thee men might in rest abide,  
We shoulden beasts bemoan, and folk thee chide,  
That where-as day with labour will us press,  
Thou thus fleest, and deignest us no rest!

Thou dost, alas! thy office too shortly,  
Thou heedless night, thy maker of mankind  
Curse thee for thy haste and unkind device,  
So fast aye to our hemisphere bind,  
That never-more under the ground thou wind!  
For now, because thou so hast risen out of Troy,  
Have I thus hastily forgone my joy!”

This Troilus, that with those words felt  
As seemed to him then, for piteous distress,  
The bloody tears from his heart did melt,  
As he that never yet such heaviness  
Assayed had, out of so great gladness,  
He therewithal Criseyde his lady dear  
In arms embraced, and said in this manner:—

“O cruel day, accuser of the joy  
That night and love have stolen and fast written,  
Accursed be thy coming into Troy,  
For every boar hath one of thy bright eyes!  
Envious day, what list thee so to spy?  
What hast thou lost, why seekest thou this place,  
Where God thy light so quench for his grace?

Alas! what have these lovers thee agilt,  
Invidious day? Thine be the pain of hell!  
For many a lover hast thou shent, and wilt;  
Thy pouring in will nowhere let them dwell.  
What profferest thou thy light here to sell?  
Go sell it them that small seals engrave;  
We will thee nought, we need no day haven.”

And eke the sun Titan gan he chide,  
And said, “O fool, well may men thee despise,  
That hast the Dawning all night at thy side,  
And suffrest her so soon up from thee rise,  
For to dis-ease lovers in this wise;  
What! hold thy bed there, thou, and eke thy morrow!  
I bid God, so give you both sorrow!”

Therewith full sore he sighed, and thus he said,  
“My lady right, and of my weal and woe  
The well and root, O goodly mine, Criseyde,  
And shall I rise, alas! and shall I go?  
Now feel I that my heart must be asunder!  
For how should I my life an hour save,  
Since that with you is all the life I have?

What shall I do, for certes, I not how,  
Nor when, alas! I shall the time see  
That in this plight I may be eft with you;  
And of my life, God wot how that shall be,  
Since that desire right now so biteth me,  
That I am dead anon, but I returne.  
How should I long, alas! from you sojourn?

But nevertheless, my own lady bright,  
Yet were it so that I wist utterly,  
That I, your humble servant and your knight,  
Were in your heart set so firmly  
As ye in mine,—which thing, truly,  
Me were more dear than these worldes twain,  
Yet should I better endure all my pain.”

To that Criseyde answered right anon,  
And with a sigh she said, “O heart dear,  
The game, y-wis, so far forth now is gone,  
That first shall Phoebus fall from out his sphere,  
And every eagle be the dove’s peer,  
And every rock out of his place start,  
Ere Troilus out of Criseyde’s heart!

Ye be so deep engraved in my heart,  
That, though I would it turn out of my thought,  
As truly, verily, God my soul save,  
To die in the pain, I could not!  
And for the love of God that us hath wrought,  
Let in your brain no other fantasy  
So creep, that it cause me to die!

And that ye me would have as fast in mind  
As I have you, that would I you beseech;  
And, if I wist soothly to find that,  
God might not a point my joys eclipse!  
But, heart of mine, withouten more speech,  
Be thou to me true, or else were it pity;  
For I am thine, by God and by my troth!

Be glad therefore, and live in sickness;  
Thus said I never ere this, nor shall to more;  
And if to you it were a great gladness  
To turn again, soon after that ye go,  
As fain would I as ye, it were so,  
As truly God my heart bring at rest!”  
And him in arms she took, and often kissed.

Against his will, since it must needs be,  
This Troilus up rose, and fast him clothed,  
And in his arms took his lady free  
An hundred times, and on his way him sped,  
And with such words as his heart bled  
He said, “Farewell, my dear heart sweet,  
There God us grant sound and soon to meet!”

To which no word for sorrow she answered;  
So sore began his parting her constrain;  
And Troilus unto his palace fared,  
As woe-begone as she was, sooth to say;  
So hard him wrong of sharp desire the pain  
To be again where he was in pleasure,  
That it may never out of his remembrance.

Returned to his royal palace, soon  
He softly into his bed began to slink,  
To sleep long, as he was wont to do.  
But all for nought; he may well lie and wink,  
But sleep ne may there in his heart sink;  
Thinking how she, for whom desire him brent,  
A thousandfold was worth more than he thought.

And in his thought began up and down to wind  
Her words all, and every countenance,  
And firmly impress in his mind  
The least point that to him was pleasure;  
And verily, of that remembrance,  
Desire all new him burned, and lust to breed  
Began more than erst, and yet took he none heed.

Criseyde also, right in the same wise,  
Of Troilus began in her heart to set  
His worthiness, his lust, his deeds’ wise,  
His gentleness, and how she with him met,  
Thanking love that so well her beset;  
Desiring again to have her heart dear  
In such a plight, she dared to make him cheer.

Pandare, a-morrow that had come at last,  
Unto his niece, and gan her fair greet,  
Said, “All this night so rained it, alas!  
That all my dread is that ye, niece sweet,  
Have little leisure had to sleep and eat;  
All night,” quoth he, “hath rain so do me wake,  
That some of us, I trow, our heads ache.”

And near he came, and said, “How standeth it now  
This merry morn, niece, how can ye fare?”  
Criseyde answered, “Never the better for you.  
Fox that ye be, God give your heart care!  
God help me so, ye caused all this fare.  
I trow,” quoth she, “for all your words so white;  
O! who-so seeth you knoweth you full little!”

Criseyde strove her blushing face to hide  
Behind the bed, and grew for shame all red;  
But Pandare underneath the bedclothes pried,  
“Dear Criseyde, if I must die,” he said,  
“Have here a sword and smite off my poor head!”  
He thrust his arm beneath her neck to twist,  
The covers off, and then his niece he kissed.

No need to tell how they were reconciled.  
If God forgave his death, then should not she  
Forgive her uncle? Thus the time they whiled  
Away in great amicability,  
As good friends now as any man could be,  
Till in good time to her own house she went,  
And left her uncle very well content.

To Troilus let us now turn again,  
Who long abed in wakeful tossing lay;  
For Pandare soon he sent some of his men  
To bid him hasten thither right away,  
And Pandare came without a nay,  
And greeting him in manner dignified,  
Upon his bed he sat down at his side.

And Troilus, moved by the deep affection  
Which for his friend within his heart now lies,  
Fell on his knees in absolute subjection,  
Nor from that humble place would he arise,  
But thanked with grateful thanks multiplied,  
A thousand times, and oft the day did bless  
That such a friend was born to save him from distress.

“O friend,” he said, “of friends the very best  
That ever was or ever was heard tell,  
Thou hast in heaven brought my soul to rest  
From Phlegethon, the fiery flood of hell;  
A thousand times a day if I should sell  
Myself to serve and honour only thee,  
Enough reward and pay it would not be.

“The sun, which moves above in all man’s sight,  
Saw never yet, this dare I well aver,  
A fairer than my dearest lady bright,  
And to my death I shall be bound to her;  
The thanks for all this favour I refer  
To Love, who honours me with kind assistance,  
And also, Pandare, to thy wise persistence.

“What thou hast given is no little thing,  
And I shall pay thee thanks forever and aye!  
And why? Because thy faithful help did bring  
Me back to life, who else were dead this day”—  
And then upon his bed again he lay.

## BOOK III

> Soberly Pandar listened at his side  
> Till he was through, and thus replied:
>
> 232
>
> “My dearest friend, if aught I’ve done for thee,  
> God knows it is to me a great relief,  
> And I’m as glad of it as you can be.  
> But now take heed that we come not to grief,  
> For there is danger still of this mischief.
>
> 225
>
> With that she gan hir face for to wrye  
> With the shete, and wex for shame al reed;  
> And Pandarus gan under for to prye,  
> And seyde, “unce, if that I shal ben deed,  
> Have here a swerd, and smyteth of myn heed.”  
> With that his arm al sodeynly he thriste  
> Under hir nekke, and at the laste hir kiste.
>
> 226
>
> I passe al that which chargeth nought to seye.  
> What! God foryaf his deeth, and she al-so  
> Foryaf, and with hir uncle gan to pleye.  
> For other cause was ther noon than so.  
> But of this thing right to the effect to go.
>
> Whan tyme was, horn til hir hous she wente.  
> And Pandarus hath fully his entente.
>
> 227
>
> Now tome we ayein to Troilus,  
> That restelees ful longe a-bedde lay.  
> And prevely sente after Pandarus,  
> To him to come in al the haste he may.  
> He com anoon, nought ones seyde he “nay,”  
> And Troilus ful sobrely he grette,  
> And doun upon his beddes syde him sette.
>
> 228
>
> This Troilus, with al the affeccioun  
> Of frendes love that herte may devyse,  
> To Pandarus on knees fil adoun.  
> And er that he wolde of the place aryse,  
> He gan him thonken in his beste wyse;  
> A hondred sythe he gan the tyme blesse,  
> That he was bom to bringe him fro distresse.
>
> 229
>
> He seyde, “O frend, of frendes th’ alderbeste  
> That ever was, the sothe for to telle,  
> Thou hast in hevene y-brought my soule at reste  
> Fro Flegiton, the fery flood of helle;  
> That, though I mighte a thousand tymes selle,  
> Upon a day, my lyf in thy servyse,  
> It mighte nought a mote in that suffyse.
>
> 230
>
> The sonne, which that al the world may see,  
> Saw never yet, my lyf, that dar I leye,  
> So inly fair and goodly as is she,  
> Whos I am al, and shal, til that I deye;  
> And, that I thus am hires, dar I seye,  
> That thanked be the heighe worthinesse  
> Of love, and eek thy kinde bisinesse.
>
> 231
>
> Thus hastow me no litel thing y-yive,  
> For which to thee obliged be for ay  
> My lyf, and why? for thorugh thyn help I live;  
> For elles deed hadde I be many a day.”  
> And with that word doun in his bed he lay,  
> And Pandarus ful sobrely him herde  
> Til al was seyd, and thanne he him answerde:
>
> 232
>
> “My dere frend, if I have doon for thee  
> In any cas, God wot, it is me leef;  
> And am as glad as man may of it be,  
> God help me so; but tak now not a-greef  
> That I shal seyn, be war of this myscheef.
>
> 233-240
>
> That, there-as thou now brought art in-to blisse.  
> That thou thy-self ne cause it nought to misse.
>
> 233
>
> For of fortunes sharp adversite  
> The worst kinde of infortune is this,  
> A man to have ben in prosperitee,  
> And it remembren, whan it passed is.  
> Thou art wys y-nough, for-thy do nought amis;  
> Be not to rakcl, though thou sitte warme.  
> For if thou be, certeyn, it wol thee harme.
>
> 234
>
> Thou art at esc, and hold thee wel ther-inne.  
> For also seur as reed is every fyr,  
> As greet a craft is kepe wel as winne;  
> Brydle alwey wel thy speche and thy desyr.  
> For worldly joye halt not but by a wyr;  
> That preveth wel, it brest alday so ofte;  
> For-thy nede is to werke with it softe.”
>
> 235
>
> Quod Troilus, “I hope, and God tofram,  
> My dere frend, that I shal so me bere,  
> That in my gilt ther shal no thing be lorn,  
> N’ I nil not rakle as for to greven here;  
> It nedeth not this matere ofte tere;  
> For wistestow myn herte wel, Pandare,  
> God wot, of this thou woldest litel care.”
>
> 236
>
> Tho gan he telle him of his glade night,  
> And wher-of first his herte dredde, and how.  
> And seyde, “freend, as I am trewe knight,  
> And by that feyth I shal to God and yow,  
> I hadde it never half so hote as now;  
> And ay the more that desyr me byteth  
> To love hir best, the more it me delyteth.
>
> 237
>
> I noot my-self not wisly what it is;  
> But now I fele a newe qualitee,  
> Ye, al another than I dide er this.”
> Pandare answerde, and seyde thus, that he  
> That ones may in hevene blisse be,  
> He feleth other weyes, dar I leye,  
> Than thilke tyme he first herde of it seye.
>
> 238
>
> This is o word for al; this Troilus  
> Was never ful, to speke of this matere.  
> And for to preysen un-to Pandarus  
> The bountee of his righte lady dere,  
> And Pandarus to thanke and maken chere.  
> This tale ay was span-newe to biginne  
> Til that the night departed hem a-twinne.
>
> 239
>
> Sone after this, for that fortune it wolde,  
> I-comen was the blisful tyme swete,  
> That Troilus was warned that he sholde,  
> Ther he was erst, Criseyde his lady mete;  
> For which he felte his herte in joye flete;  
> And feythfully gan alle the goddes herie;  
> And lat see now if that he can be merie.
>
> 240
>
> And holden was the forme and al the wyse,  
> Of hir cominge, and eek of his also,  
> As it was erst, which nedeth nought devyse.

---

> That now that thou art settled in thy bliss,  
> Thyself may cause affairs to go amiss.
>
> 233
>
> “Of fickle fortune’s sharp adversities.  
> The very worst misfortune of them all,  
> Is this, to know and lose all joy and ease,  
> And have but bitter memories to recall.  
> Exert thy wisdom such fate to forestall;  
> Be not too rash, nor of thyself too sure,  
> Or harm will quickly come and long endure.
>
> 234
>
> “Thou art at ease, and hold thee well therein,  
> For just as true that red is every fire,  
> To keep demands as much skill as to win.  
> Then bridle well thy speech and thy desire,  
> For worldly joys hang by a subtle wire,  
> And for sad proof, it breaketh quick and oft,  
> Wherefore the need to walk both light and soft.
>
> 235
>
> “I hope,” said Troilus, “before men’s eves,  
> Dear friend, that I such heed shall take,  
> That through my fault no danger shall arise,  
> And rashness I abjure for her dear sake.  
> You need not fear my promise I shall break,  
> If you but knew the secrets of my mind,  
> Then mighty little cause for fear you’d find.”
>
> 236
>
> And then he told him of his happy night,  
> And how at first he was afraid, and why,  
> And said, “I swear upon my honor bright  
> And by my faith in you and God on high,  
> I never knew what loving did imply;  
> For as my heart’s desires rose in height,  
> The greater grew my love and my delight.
>
> 237
>
> “To me myself it is a mystery,  
> For now I feel in me a nature new,  
> A thing that makes a different man of me.”
> And Pandar said, “Yes, I suppose it’s true,  
> That he who once in heaven’s bliss may be,  
> He feels it all in quite another way  
> Than when he knew it only by hear-say.”
>
> 238
>
> But now enough— though Troilus indeed  
> To speak of this doth never stop or tire,  
> And still to praise his lady would proceed.  
> Exalting all her bounty higher and higher,  
> And thanking Pandar all he could require,  
> Then in again bran-new he ever starts,  
> Until his friend at night homeward departs.
>
> 239
>
> Soon after this, by great good luck, it fell  
> He had a chance his night-watch to repeat,  
> For Pandar came the happy news to tell,  
> That Cressida, his lady, he should meet.  
> How then his heart with sudden joy doth beat!  
> His thanks to all the Gods he then did pay,  
> And you can guess if he was glad and gay!
>
> 240
>
> The manner of this meeting was again  
> Somewhat as I have told and as you know,  
> And so I shall not bother to explain,  
> But to the end now let us straightway go;  
> For Pandar still his faithful aid did show  
> And brought them to the place they liked the best.  
> And there they are in quiet and in rest.

---

> 241
>
> You have no need, now they again are met,  
> To ask of me if they were happy there.  
> For what was good before, grows better yet  
> A thousandfold, with goodness still to spare.  
> And now they know no sorrow or no care,  
> For joy as great to them the kind Gods send  
> As any human heart may comprehend.
>
> 242
>
> This is no trifling thing that now I say,  
> ’Tis something no man’s wit can all comprise;  
> For each to other’s will doth so obey  
> That all the joys which ancient clerks so wise  
> Have praised, counted as nothing in their eyes;  
> Their joy may not be written down in ink,  
> For it surpasses all that heart may think!
>
> 243
>
> But cruel day, alack the fateful hour,  
> Again returns, as they by signs well knew,  
> And they must yield to sorrow’s greater power.  
> Full sad they were, full sad and pale of hue,  
> Reviling day with scornings ever new,  
> Calling it traitor, envious and worse—  
> O, bitterly the light of day they curse!
>
> 244
>
> “Alas,” said Troilus, “now is it plain  
> That Pyroeis and his team-mates three,  
> Which draw the bright sun’s chariot in their train,  
> Have gone some short cut in despite of me,  
> And that is why the night so soon doth flee;  
> And if the sun will hasten thus the day,  
> No offerings on his altar shall I lay.”
>
> 245
>
> But day must come, and they must separate,  
> And after all was said that could be said,  
> They finally submit to their sad fate,  
> Yet for a meeting set a time ahead;  
> And thus for many a night their lives they led  
> As Fortune gave to them this ample joy,  
> To Cressida and Troilus of Troy.
>
> 246
>
> In great content, in sport and merry songs,  
> Troilus passes now his happy days.  
> He spends, he jousts, his feastings he prolongs,  
> Himself in gaudy garments he arrays,  
> He has a world of folk about always,  
> The freshest and the best that he can find,  
> As suiting one of his so noble kind.
>
> 247
>
> Such name and fame of him now circulate  
> Throughout the world, his honor and largess,  
> It mounts and rings at heaven’s very gate;  
> And through his love he knows so great gladness
