Down from their horses quickly they alight,  
And to the room of Troilus go straight,  
And sit them down and talk till almost night.  
Which talk was all of Cressida the bright,  
And afterward, when so they felt inclined,  
They went to bed, though first, of course, they dined.

76

Next day, before the morning lights shone clear,  
Troilus awoke, and leaping from his bed,  
He roused Pandar out, his brother dear,  
“For love of God,” most plaintively he said,  
“I cannot rest until I’ve visited  
Her house, and though my eyes I cannot feast  
On her, I can her palace see at least.”

77

He found some way his household to mislead,  
And he and Pandar to the town then go,  
And to his lady’s house at once proceed,  
But, Lord, he only hastened to his woe!  
He thought his heart with grief must overflow,  
For when he saw the doors still bolted tight,  
Upon his horse he scarce could sit upright.

78

For with one fatal glance his eyes beheld  
That shut was every window of the place,  
And at the sight his heart like ice grew cold;  
Without a word, and deadly pale of face,  
Forth by the palace did he madly race;  
He spurs his horse and rides away full speed,  
And of no man he takes the slightest heed.

79

“O palace desolate,” he then began,  
“O house, of houses once most dear to sight!  
O palace, empty and accursed of man!  
O lantern, wherein now is quenched the light!  
O dwelling, once my day, now turned to night!  
Why dost thou stand, while all my joys decay,  
And she is gone, who was my hope and stay.

80

“O palace, once the crown of houses all,  
Illumined with the sun of every bliss!  
O ring, from which the ruby now doth fall!  
O cause of woe, but cause of joy ere this!  
Yet lacking better, fain now would I kiss  
Thy doorways cold, if folk were not about!  
Act farewell, shrine, from which the saint is out!”

81

On Pandar then he cast his mournful eye,  
With face all drawn and dreadful to behold,  
And interrupted oft with many a sigh,  
To him the devastating tale he told,  
Of sorrow new and former joys grown old;  
And pain sat on his countenance so grim,  
No heart so hard but must have pitied him.

82

From thenceforth he rode here and up and down,  
And everything came back in memory  
As he rode by the places in the town  
In which he once had known all his pleasure.  
“Lo, yonder saw I my own lady dance;  
And in that temple, with her eyes so clear,  
Me caught first my right lady dear.

83

And yonder have I heard full lustily  
My dear heart laugh, and yonder play  
I saw her once also full blissfully.  
And yonder once to me she came to say,  
‘Now good sweet, love me well, I pray.’  
And yonder so goodly she me beheld,  
That to the death my heart is to her held.

84

And at that corner, in that yonder house,  
I heard my most beloved lady dear  
So womanly, with voice melodious,  
Singing so well, so goodly, and so clear,  
That in my soul yet seems I hear  
The blissful sound; and, in that yonder place,  
My lady first took me unto her grace.

85

Then thought he thus, “O blissful lord Cupid,  
When I the process have in my memory,  
How thou me hast warred on every side.  
A man might make a book of it, like a story.  
What need is there for thee to seek victory over me,  
Since I am thine, and wholly at thy will?  
What joy hast thou to slay thine own folk?

86

Well hast thou, lord, avenged on me thy ire,  
Thou mighty god, and dreadful to be grieved!  
Now mercy, lord, thou knowest well I desire  
Thy grace most, of all lusts leave.  
And live and die I will in thy belief;  
For which I ask in guerdon but a boon,  
That thou Cressida again to me send soon.

87

Constrain her heart as fast to return  
As thou dost mine to long her to see;  
Then know I well, that she will not sojourn.  
Now, blissful lord, so cruel thou be not  
Unto the blood of Troy, I pray thee,  
As Juno was unto the blood of Thebes,  
For which the folk of Thebes took her bane.”

88

And after this he went to the gates  
Where Cressida had ridden out at a good pace,  
And up and down there made he many a go.  
And to himself full often he said, “Alas!  
From hence rode my bliss and my solace!  
As would blissful God now, for his joy,  
I might see her again come into Troy.

89

And to the yonder hill I guided her,  
Alas! and there I took of her my leave!  
And yonder I saw her to her father ride.  
For sorrow of which my heart shall cleave in two.  
And hither home I came when it was eve;

90

And oft imagined of himself he would be  
Made weak, and pale, and waxen less  
Than he was wont, and that men whispered soft,  
“What may it be? who can the truth guess  
Why Troilus hath all this heaviness?”  
And all this was but his melancholy,  
That he had of himself such fantasy.

91

Another time he would imagine  
That every wight that went by the way  
Had pity of him, and that they would say,  
“I am right sorry, Troilus will die.”  
And thus he drew a day yet forth or two.  
As you have heard, such life right did he lead,  
As he that stood betwixt hope and dread.

92

For which it pleased him in his songs to show  
The cause of his woe, as he best might,  
And make a song of words but a few,  
Somewhat his woeful heart for to lighten.  
And when he was from every man’s sight,  
With gentle voice he, of his lady dear,  
That was absent, began to sing as you may hear.

93

“O star, of which I have lost all the light,  
With sore heart well ought I to bewail,  
That ever dark in torment, night by night,  
Toward my death with wind in stern I sail;  
For which the tenth night if that I fail  
The guiding of thy beams bright an hour,  
My ship and me Charybdis will devour.”

94

This song when he thus had sung, soon  
He fell again into his old sighs;  
And every night, as was his wont to do,  
He stood the bright moon to behold,  
And all his sorrow he to the moon told;  
And said, “Y-wis, when thou art horned new,  
I shall be glad, if all the world be true!

95

I saw thy horns old also by the morrow,  
When hence rode my right lady dear,  
That cause is of my torment and my sorrow;  
For which, O bright Lucina the dear,  
For love of God, run fast about thy sphere!  
For when thy horns anew begin to spring,  
Then shall she come, that may my bliss bring!”

96

The day is more, and longer every night,  
Than they be wont to be, him seemed then;  
And that the sun went his course unright  
By longer way than it was wont to go;  
And said, “Y-wis, me dreadeth evermore,  
The sun’s son, Phaeton, be alive,  
And that his father’s chariot amiss he drives.”

97

“Lo, yonder is the one I love the most!  
Lo, yonder the tent whence, like a pining ghost,  
There comes this sighing breeze so gently blowing,  
New life upon my lifeless soul bestowing.  
And verily this wind, that more and more  
Increases steadily upon my face,  
Is from my lady’s deep sighs sore.

98

I prove it thus, for in no other place  
Of all this town, save only in this space,  
Feel I no wind that soundeth so like pain;  
It saith, ‘Alas! why parted are we twain?’”

99

This long time he drives forth right thus,  
Till fully passed was the ninth night;  
And ever beside him was this Pandarus,  
That busily did all his full might,  
Him to comfort, and make his heart light;  
Giving him hope always, the tenth to-morrow  
That she shall come, and stinting all his sorrow.

100

Upon the other side also was Cressida,  
With few women, among the Greeks strong;  
For which full often a day she said “Alas!  
That I was born! Woe may my heart long  
After my death; for now live I too long!  
Alas! and I cannot amend it;  
For now is worse than ever yet I wended.

101

My father will for nothing grant me grace  
To go again, for nought I can him please;  
And if so be that I my term pass,  
My Troilus shall in his heart deem  
That I am false, and so it may well seem.  
Thus shall I have ingratitude on every side;  
That I was born, so woe the time!

102

And if that I put myself in jeopardy,  
To steal away by night, and it befall  
That I be caught, I shall be held a spy;  
Or else, lo, this fear I most of all.  
If into the hands of some wretch I fall,  
I am but lost, although my heart be true;  
Now mighty God, thou on my sorrow rue!”

103

Full pale waxen was her bright face,  
Her limbs lean, as she that all the day  
Stood when she durst, and looked on the place  
Where she was born, and where she had dwelt always.  
And all the night weeping, “Alas!” she lay.  
And thus despairing, out of all cure,  
She led her life, this woeful creature.

104

Full often a day she sighed also for distress,  
And in herself she went aye portraying  
Of Troilus the great worthiness,  
And all his goodly words recording  
Since first that day her love began to spring.  
And thus she set her woeful heart afire  
Through remembrance of that she began to desire.

105

Full ruefully she looked upon Troy,  
Beheld the towers high and also the halls;  
“Allas!” said she, “the pleasure and the joy  
Which that now all turned into gall is.  
Have I had oft within those walks!  
O Troilus, what dost thou now,” she said;  
“Lord! whether yet thou thinkest upon Cressida?

106

Alas! I had not believed in your counsel,  
And gone with you, as ye advised me ere this!  
Then had I now not sighed half so sore.  
Who might have said that I had done amiss  
To steal away with such one as he is?  
But all too late cometh the letting,  
When men the corpse unto the grave carry.

107

Too late is now to speak of this matter;  
Prudence, alas! one of thy three eyes  
Me lacked always, ere that I came here;  
The time past, well remembered by me;  
And present time also could I well see.  
But future time, ere I was in the snare,  
Could I not see; that causeth now my care.

108

But nevertheless, betide what betide,  
I shall tomorrow at night, by east or west,  
Out of this host steal on some manner side,  
And go with Troilus wheresoever him list.  
This purpose will I hold, and this is best.  
No heed of wicked tongues janglery,  
For every one in love hath wretches envied.

109

For who-so will of every word take heed,  
Or rule himself by every wight’s wit,  
Shall never thrive, out of dread.  
For that that some men blame ever yet,  
Lo, other manner folk commend it.  
And as for me, for all such variance,  
Happiness I call my sufficiency.

110

For which, withouten any words more,  
To Troy I will, as for conclusion.”  
But God it wot, ere fully months two,  
She was full far from that intention.  
For both Troilus and Troy town  
Shall knottles through-out her heart slide;  
For she will take a purpose for to bide.

111

This Diomede, of whom I told before,  
Goes now, within himself ever arguing  
With all the sleight and all that ever he can,  
How he may best, with shortest tarrying,  
Into his net Cressida’s heart bring.  
To this intent he could never fine;  
To fish her, he laid out hook and line.

# BOOK V

> But natheles, wel in his herte he thoughte,  
> That she nas nat with-oute a love in Troye.  
> For never, sithen he hir thennes broughte,  
> Ne coude he seen her laughe or make joye.  
> He niste how best hir herte for t'acoye.  
> “But for t'assaye,” he seyde, “it nought ne greveth;  
> For he that nought n'assayeth, nought n'acheveth.”

> Yet seide he to him-self upon a night,  
> “Now am I not a fool, that woot wel how  
> Hir wo for love is of another wight,  
> And here up-on to goon assaye hir now?  
> I may wel wite, it nil not been my prow.  
> For wysc folk in bokes it expresse,  
> ‘Men shal not wowe a wight in hevinesse.’  

> But who-so mighte winnen swich a flour  
> From him, for whom she morneth night and day,  
> He mighte seyn, he were a conquerour.”  

> And right anoon, as he that bold was ay,  
> Thoughte in his herte, “happe, how happe may,  
> Al sholde I deye, I wole hir herte seche;  
> I shal no more lesen but my speche.”

> This Diomede, as bokes us declare,  
> Was in his nedes prest and corageous;  
> With sterne voys and mighty lymes square.  
> Hardy, testif, strong, and chevalrous  
> Of dedes, lyk his fader Tideus.  
> And som men seyn, he was of tunge large;  
> And heir he was of Calidoine and Arge.

> Criseyde mene was of hir stature,  
> Ther-to of shap, of face, and eek of chere,  
> Ther mighte been no fairer creature.  
> And ofte tyme this was hir manere,  
> To gon y-tressed with hir heres clere  
> Doun by hir coler at hir bak bihinde,  
> Which with a threde of gold she wolde binde.

> And, save hir browes joyneden y-fere,  
> Ther nas no lak, in ought I can espyen;  
> But for to speken of hir eyen clere,  
> Lo, trewely, they writen that hir syen,  
> That Paradys stood formed in hir yen.  
> And with hir riche beautee ever-more  
> Strof love in hir, ay which of hem was more.

> She sobre was, eek simple, and wys with-al,  
> The beste y-norisshed eek that mighte be.  
> And goodly of hir speche in general,  
> Charitable, estatliche, lusty, and free;  
> Ne never-mo ne lakkede hir pitee;  
> Tendre-herted, slydinge of corage;  
> But trewely, I can not telle hir age.

> And Troilus wel waxen was in highte,  
> And complet formed by proporcioun  
> So wel, that kinde it not amenden mighte;

1 12

But natheless, well in his heart he thought
That she was not without some love in Troy.
For never, since he her from thence had brought,
Could he behold her laugh, or see her joy.
He knew not how her heart to ease or cloy.
“But for to try,” he said, “it shall not grieve;
For he that nothing ventures, nothing will achieve.”

1 13

And so he said unto himself one night,
“Now am I not a fool, that see full well
How her distress is for another wight;
And shall I, now, upon her bosom dwell?
I wot full well, it will not be my spell.
For wise folk in their books this lesson write,
‘Men shall not woo a wight in heaviness.’”

1 14

But who might win so fair a flow’r away
From him for whom she mourns both night and day,
He might well say, he were a conqueror.”

And straight anon, as he that ever dared,
Thought in his heart, “Happen what may befall,
Though I should die, her heart I will assay;
I shall lose nothing save my words most small.”

1 15

This Diomede, as books and tales declare,
Was ready in his needs and courageous;
With stern voice, mighty limbs and square of frame.
Hardy and brave, strong, chevalrous,
Of deeds like to his father Tydeus old;
Some say he was of speech both large and bold;
He was of Calydon and Argos’ fold.

1 16

Cressida was even of her stature,
And of her shape, her face, and eke her cheer;
In all her parts no fairer creature nature
Had framed, that man might ever see or hear.
And oftentimes it was her manner clear
To wear her hair in tresses winding low
Down by her collar at her back, y-plaited so,
Which with a thread of gold she would a-bind.

1 17

And, save her brows that joined at the mind,
There was no blemish that my eye could find;
But to speak of her eyes so bright and clear,
Lo, truly they did write within her sight
That Paradise itself was fashioned there.
And with her rich beauty evermore
Strove Love within her, aye contending sore
Which of them should be more.

1 18

She sober was, and simple, and wise alway,
The best reared that ever might be found;
And goodly of her speech in general,
Charitable, stately, lusty, and free;
Nor ever lacked she pity’s kindly sound;
Tender of heart, slow-moving of courage;
But truly, I can not declare her age.

1 19

And Troilus was waxen well in height,
And fully formed by proportion right
So well, that nature could not mend aright;

1 20

Young, fresh, strong, and hardy as a lion;
True as to steel in every condition;
One of the best endowed creature,
That is, or shall, while that the world may dure.

1 20 (alternate Middle English stanza)

And certainly in story it is found,
That Troilus was never to no wight
In his time in any degree second
In doings that belong to any knight.
Al might a giant pass him in mere might,
His heart was ever like the first and best,
Stood par equal in deeds, to do his best.

1 21

But, for to tellen forth of Diomede: —
It fell that after, on the tenth day
Since that Cressida out of the city yede,
This Diomede, as fresh as bough in May,
Came to the tent where Calchas old did lay,
And feigned him that to Calchas he had to do;
But what he meant, I shall you tell anon.

1 22

Cressida, at short words to tell,
Welcomed him, and down beside her him set;
And he was easy enough to make to dwell.
And after this, withouten long delay,
The spices and the wine men forth hem fet;
And forth they spake of this and that together,
As friends do, of which some shall ye hear.

1 23

He began first upon the war in speech
Betwix them and the folk of Troy town;
And of the siege he did her eke beseech
To tell him what was her opinion.
From that demand he so descended down
To ask her if that her strange thought
The Greeks’ guise, and works that they wrought?

1 24

And why her father tarried so long
To wedde her unto some worthy wight?
Cressida, who was in her pains strong
For love of Troilus, her own knight,
As far forth as she knowledge had or might,
Answered him then; but, as of his intent,
It seemed not she wist what he it meant.

1 25

But natheless, this same Diomede
Gained assurance in himself, and thus he said,
“If I aright have taken heed of you,
Me thinketh thus, O lady mine, Cressida,
That since I first hand upon your bridle laid,
When you came out of Troy upon the morrow,
Never could I see you but in sorrow.

1 26

I can not say what may the cause be,
But if for love of some Trojan it were;
Which right sore would reckon with me,
That I, for any wight that dwelleth there,
Should spill a quarter of a tear,
Or pitifully yourselves so beguile;
For doubtless, it is not worth the while.

1 27

The folk of Troy, as who sayeth, all and some
Are prisoners, and never shall be free,
For out of Troy not one alive shall come,
For all the gold between the sun and sea.
Trust well, and understandeth me,
There shall not one to mercy go alive,
Although he were lord of worlds twice five!

1 28

Such wreche on them for fetching of Eleyne
Shall be taken ere that we hence wend;
That Manes, which be gods of pain,
Shall be aghast that Greeks should them offend;
And men shall dread it to the world’s end,
Henceforth to ravish any queen;
So cruel shall our vengeance on them be seen.

1 29

And but-if Calchas lead us with ambages,
That is to say, with double words sly,
Such as men clepe a ‘word with two visages,’
Ye shall well know that I not lie;
And all this thing right soon ye shall espie,
And that anon; ye will not believe how soon;
Now taketh heed, for it is for to do.

1 30

What wene ye your wise father would
Have given Antenor for you anon,
If he ne wist that the city should
Be destroyed? Why nay, so mot I gon!
He knew full well there shall not scape one
That is Trojan; and for the great fear,
He durst not, ye dwelt longer there.
What will ye more, love-some lady dear?

Let Troy and Trojans from your heart pace!
Drive out that bitter hope, and make good cheer.
And call again the beauty of your face,
That ye with sacked tears so deface.

For Troy is brought in such a jeopardy
That to save it now is no remedy.

1 32

And thenketh well, ye shall in Greeks find
A more perfect love, ere it be night,
Than any Trojan is, and more kind,
And better to serve you will do his might.
And if ye vouchsafe, my lady bright,
I will be he to serve you myself;
Yea, rather than be lord of Greces twelve!

1 33

And with that word he gan to waxen red,
And in his speech a little while he quook,
And cast aside a little while his head,
And stinted awhile; and afterward awook.
And soberly on her he threw his look,
And said, “I am, albeit it you no joy,
As gentle man as any wight in Troy.

1 34

For if my fader Tydeus,” he said,
“Had lived longer, I had been, er this,
Of Calydon and Argos a king, Cressida!
And so hope I that I shall yet, y-wis.
But he was slain, alas! the more harm is,
Unhappily at Thebes all to rathe,
Polynices and many a man to scathe.

1 35

But, lady dear, since now I am your man,
And in my heart you hold the chiefest place,
To serve you as heartily as I can,
And ever shall, while I to live have space,
So, ere that I depart out of this place,
Ye will me grant that I may tomorrow,
At better leisure, tell you of my sorrow.”

1 36

What should I tell the pretty speeches he said?
He spake enough for one day at the least;
It proves well, he spake so that Cressida
Granted, on the morrow, to his request,
To speak with him at the least,
So that he would not speak of such matter;
And thus to him she said, as ye may hear:

1 37

As she that had her heart on Troilus
So fast, that there might it none unlace;
And strangely she spake, and said thus,
“O Diomede, I love that ilke place
Where I was born; and Jove, for his grace,
Deliver it soon from all that doth it care!
God, for thy might, so leave it well to fare!

1 38

That Greeks would wreak their wrath on Troy,
If that they might, I know it well, y-wis.
But it shall not befall as ye do say;
And God forbid that such an altered bliss!
I know my father did to him me sell,
And that he dearly bought me, as ye say;
And for all this, I shall him well repay.

1 39

That Greeks be folk of high condition,
I know eek well; but certes, men shall find
As worthy folk within Troy town,
As cunning, and as perfect and as kind,
As be betwixt the Orkades and Inde.
And that some lady gladly would receive
Your service, that I’m ready to believe.

1 40

But as for love,” she said, and gently sighed,
“I had a lord, and I his wedded wife,
To whom my heart was pledged until he died;
But other love than that in all my life
There hath not been, nor shall I seek love’s strife.
And that you are of high and noble birth,
That have I heard, and know you for your worth.

1 41

And for that reason now I wonder,
That any man should trouble any woman so.
Eek, God wot, love and I be far asunder;
I am disposed, so mote I go,
Until my death, to plain and make my woe.
What I shall after do, I cannot say;
But truly, as yet me list not to play.

1 42

My heart is now in tribulation,
And you in arms are busy day by day.
Hereafter, when you have won the town,
Perchance, then so it happen may,
That when I see that I never ere saw,
Then will I work those things I never wrought.
This word to you enough should suffice.

1 43

Tomorrow eke I will speak with you fain,
So that ye touch nought of this matter;
And when you list, you may come here again;
And, ere you go, thus much I say you here:
As help me Pallas with her hair’s clear sheen,
If that I should of any Greek have ruth,
It should be yourself, by my truth!

1 44

I say not therefore that I will you love,
Nor I say not nay; but in conclusion
I mean well, by God that sits above!” —
And therewithal she cast her eyen down,
And gan to sigh, and said, “O Troy town,
Yet bid I God, in quiet and in rest
I may you see, or do my heart to brest.”

1 45

But in effect, and shortly for to say,
This Diomede all freshly new again
Gan press on, and fast her mercy pray;
And after this, the truth for to say,
Her glove he took, of which he was full fain,
And finally, when it waxed eve,
And all was well, he rose and took his leave.

1 46

The bright Venus followed and aye taught
The way where broad Phoebus down alight;
And Cynthia her chariot-horses over-raught
To whirl out of the Lion, if she might;
And Signifer his candles showed bright.
When that Cressida unto her bed went
Within her father’s fair and shining tent,

1 47

Returning in her soul aye up and down
The words of this sudden Diomede,
His great estate, and peril of the town,
And that she was alone and had great need
Of friend’s help; and thus began to breed
The cause why, the truth for to tell,
That she took fully purpose for to dwell.

1 48

The morrow came, and ghostly for to speak,
This Diomede is come unto Cressida,
And shortly, lest that ye my tale break,
So well he for himself spake and said,
That all her sighs sore down he laid,
And finally, the truth for to say,
He reft her of the great of all her pain.

1 49

And after this the story telleth us,
That she him gave the fair bay steed,
Which once he won of Troilus;
And eke a brooch (and that was little need)
That Troilus was, she gave this Diomede.
And eke, the better from sorrow him to relieve,
She made him wear a pennon of her sleeve.

1 50

I find eke in the stories elsewhere,
When deep wounded once was Diomede
By Troilus, she wept tears manifold
When that she saw his wide wounds bleed;
And that she took to keep him good heed,
And for to heal him of his sorrows smart,
Men say, men say, that she gave him her heart.

1 51

And yet the story also telleth us,
There made never woman more wo
Than she, when that she falsed Troilus.
She said, “Alas! for now is clean ago
My name of troth in love, for ever-mo!
For I have falsed one, the gentlest
That ever was, and one the worthiest!

1 52

Alas, of me unto the world’s end,
Shall neither be written nor be song
No good word, for these books will me shend;
O, rolled shall I be on many a tongue!
Throughout the world my bell shall be rung;
And women most will hate me of all.
Alas, that such a case on me should fall!

1 53

And they will say, in scorning of all this,
That I dishonored them, alack the day!
Though I were not the first that did amiss,
That will not wipe the blot of shame away.
But since what’s done, must so for ever stay,
And since my former guilt I can’t undo,
To Diomede at least I shall be true.

1 54

But Troilus, since I can do no more,
And since our paths henceforth must separate,
May heaven to its favor thee restore!
O Troilus, the best and gentlest mate
Who e’er his heart to love did consecrate,
What other love can stand in thy dear stead!”

She brake, and bitter tears she forth did shed.

1 55

“Of this I’m sure, that I shall hate you never,
A friend’s love you shall have at least of me,
And my good word, though I should live forever!
And truly I should grieve if I should see
You ever fall into adversity.
That you are guiltless, no one need me tell;
God’s will be done! And thus I say farewell!”

1 56

How long a time it was that lay between
Ere she forsook him for bold Diomede,
No author tells, so far as I have seen,
And no man, let him never so widely read,
Shall find a further record of this deed;
But Diomede, though quick enough to woo,
Before he won her, yet more had to do.

1 57

Nor shall I now this woman further chide
Than from her simple story doth arise;
Her name, alas, is published far and wide,
Her guilt is plain enough to all men’s eyes.

---

[120-127 (alternate/translation stanzas)]

But he was wary, since he surely thought
That she had left some love in Troy behind;
For ever since she from that town was brought,
She seemed to carry something on her mind,
Some loss to which she could not be resigned.
“But still,” he said, “to try is worth the pains,
For he who nothing ventures, nothing gains.”

And so he said unto himself one day,
“Now I am not a fool—I see full well
How she is sad because her lover’s away;
If I should press her now, it would not dwell.
I must give time; wise folk in books do tell,
‘Do not make love to those in great distress.’”

“But such a flower for yourself to win
From him for whom she mourneth night and day,
That were a conquest one might pride to pin!”
And boldly then, for he delayed no way,
“Let happen,” he declared, “whatever may;
I’ll try her out, and if she should refuse,
I’ve nothing but a little breath to lose.”

This Diomede, as all the books attest,
Was quick in action, also brave and bold,
And stern of voice, with mighty arms and chest.
For deeds adventurous he was extolled,
High as his father Tydeus of old;
Some say his speech could reach with loftier range—
This prince of Argos and of Calydon strange.

Cressida was even of her stature,
And in her shape, her look, and all her face,
No fairer creature ever blessed man’s sight.
Following custom of her time and place,
She wore her hair braided in a lace,
Down by her collar at her back behind,
And with a thread of gold she did it bind.

Her curving brows beneath her forehead met,
And in all things men counted her most fair;
Her eyes within their frames were brightly set,
And all who saw her with one voice declare
That Paradise in truth was written there;
Beauty and love in her were so create
That which was greater one could but debate.

Sedate she was, simple and wise withal,
Instructed in the arts most carefully,
Goodly of speech, whatever might befall,
With kindly grace, both dignified and free;
Nor lacked her heart in sense of sympathy
In all the things which kindly feeling moves,
But I regret I cannot tell her loves.

And Troilus was more than middle height,
But well-proportioned, of figure neat;
In short, he seemed in everything just right,
Young, fresh, and quick as lion on his feet,
And true as steel his heart within its seat;
He was with all the qualities endowed
That to our human nature are allowed.

In all the histories it is related
That Troilus was never in men’s sight
In lower rank than with the highest rated,
In noble deeds pertaining to a knight;
Though not a giant in his body’s might,
His heart was ever equal to the best
In deeds that knightly competence attest.

But now let us return to Diomede.
The tenth day came since that sad parting day
When to the Greeks this lady he did lead.
And Diomede, fresh as flowers in May,
Came to the tent where wise old Calchas lay,
And feigned that he had business with the priest,
But of his plans, the business was the least.

Now Cressida, in all things neat and nice,
Received him there, and bade him take a seat,
Nor had she any need to ask him twice;
And in the proper way a guest to treat,
Spices and wine she served in manner meet;
In friendly conversation then they fell,
A part of which I shall proceed to tell.

First of the war he then began to speak
Between the Greeks and the besieged at Troy,
And her opinion humbly he did seek
What methods in the siege one should employ;
And then he asked if she did there enjoy
Her life among the Greeks, and if their ways
Seemed strange, and how she passed her further days.

And why her father should delay so long
To marry her unto some good worthy knight.
But Cressida, who felt the pain so strong
For absent Troilus, her heart’s delight,
Gave answer to his questions as she might,
But of his deeper purpose and intent
Perhaps she had no inkling what he meant.

But nevertheless the dauntless Diomede
Pressed bravely on, and this attempt essayed:
“If I have rightly of you taken heed,
Dear Cressida, I’m very much afraid,
Since hand upon your bridle first I laid,
When you came forth from Troy upon that morrow,
You have been sore oppressed by some deep sorrow.

“I cannot say just what the cause may be,
Unless perchance some Trojan you hold dear;
Yet let me say, it truly would grieve me
If you for any Trojan, far or near,
Should ever spill a quarter of a tear,
Or let one smile from off your face depart,
For, Cressida, 'tis not worth while in heart.

“The Trojans, one might say, both all and some,
Are prisoners, and never shall be free;
For out of Troy not one alive shall come
For all the gold between the sun and sea.
Trust well, and understandeth this of me,
There shall not one to mercy go alive,
Although he were the lord of worlds twice five!

“The rape of Helen shall so be repaid,
Ere we upon our homeward way shall wend;
The Manes, gods of pain, shall stand afraid
Lest Grecian wrath with theirs should e’er contend;
And men shall dread it to the world’s end,
Henceforth henceforth to ravish any queen;
Such vengeance on the Trojans shall be seen.

“And but if Calchas lead us with ambages,
That is, with double words of subtle art,
Such as men call a ‘word with two visages,’
Ye shall well know that I do not depart
From truth; and all this ye shall see with your heart,
And that anon; ye will not trow how soon;
Now take good heed, for this is to be done.

“What think ye your wise father would have given
To Antenor for you, if he had known
That the city should be overthrown?
Why, nay—so may I go! He knew full well
There shall not one escape that is Trojan;
And for the great fear he durst not suffer ye
To dwell the longer there. What will ye plea?

“Let Troy and Trojans from your heart be chased!
Drive out that bitter hope, and make good cheer;
And call again the beauty of your face,
Which with the salt of tears you thus deface!
For Troy is brought into such jeopardy
That, to preserve it, is now no remedy.

“And thinketh well, ye shall in Greeks find
A more perfect love, ere long this night befall,
Than any Trojan is, and more kind,
And better to serve you will do his all;
And if ye will vouchsafe me, lady bright,
I will be he to serve you—myself, by right!
Yea rather than be lord of Grece’s might!”

And with that word he blushed a bashful red,
And in his speech his little frame he shook,
And cast aside awhile his heed, and said,
And stinted a short while; then forth he took
A sober look and on her visage look,
And said, “I am, though this to you no joy,
A gentleman as any in this Troy.

“For if my father Tydeus had lived,
I had been ere now of Calydon and Argos king;
And so I hope that yet shall be achieved.
But he was slain—alas!—a grievous thing
At Thebes, where Polynices and his ring
Suffered, and many a knight was done to scathe.”

---

1 35

“But, lady dear, since now I am your man,
And being the first of those from whom I seek grace,
To serve you as heartily as ever I can,
And ever shall, while I have worldly space,
So, ere that I depart out of this place,
Ye will me grant that I may tomorrow,
At better leisure, tell you of my sorrow.”

1 36

What should I tell the words that he there said?
He spake enough for one day at the least;
It proveth well, he spake so that Cressida
Granted on the morrow at his request
To speak with him at the least,
So that he would not speak of such matter;
And thus unto him she said, as ye may hear:

1 37

As she that hadde her heart on Troilus
So fast, that there might it none unlace;
And strangely she spake, and said thus,
“O Diomede, I love that ilke place
Where I was born; and Jove, for his grace,
Deliver it soon from all that doth it care!
God, for thy might, so leave it well to fare!

1 38

That Greeks would wreak their wrath on Troy, I wot;
If that they might, I know it well, y-wis.
But it shall not befall as ye now say;
And God forbid that such ill come to this.
I wot my father is wise and ready his,
And that he me hath bought, as ye me tolde;
So dear I am unto him, the more I him holde.

1 39

That Greeks be of high condition I know well;
But for a certainty, men shall finde
As worthy folk within the Trojan town,
As cunning, and as perfect, and as kind,
As be betwixt the Orkades and Inde.
And that ye could well your lady serve,
I trow eke well, her thanks for to deserve.

1 40

But as to speak of love, y-wis,” she said,
“I had a lord, to whom I wedded was,
Whose was my heart wholly, till that he died;
And other love within my heart there nis,
Ne never was—so help me now Pallas.
And that ye be of noble and high kin,
I have well heard it told, out of no sin.

1 41

And that doth me to have such wonder,
That ye wal scorn any woman so.
Eek, God wot, love and I be far asunder;
I am disposed better, so mot I go,
Until my death, to pine and make my woe.
What I shall after do, I can not say;
But truly, as yet me lists not to play.

1 42

My heart is now in tribulation,
And ye in arms busy, day by day;
Hereafter, when ye shall have won the town,
Perchance, then so it happen may,
That when I see what I never ere saw,
Then will I work that I never wrought.
These words to you enough should suffice.

1 43

Tomorrow also will I speak with you fain,
So that ye touch not of this matter more;
And when ye list, you may come here again;
And, ere ye go, thus much I say you here:
As help me Pallas with her hairs clear-sheen,
If I should of any Greek have ruth,
It should be your-self, by my truth!

1 44

I say not therefore that I will you love,
Nor do I say nay; but in conclusion
I mean well, by God that sits above.” —
And therewith she cast her eyes down low,
And gan to sigh, and said, “O Troy town,
Yet pray I God, in quiet and in rest
I may you see, or do my heart to brest.”

1 45

But in effect, and shortly for to say,
This Diomede all freshly new again
Gan press on, and fast her mercy pray;
And after this, the truth for to say,
Her glove he took, of which he was full fain,
And finally, when it waxed toward eve,
And all was well, he rose and took his leave.

1 46

Bright Venus followed and aye taught
The way where Phoebus down his carriage light;
And Cynthia her chariot-horses over-wrought
To whirl out of the Lion, if she might;
And Signifer his candles showed bright.
When Cressida unto her bed went
Within her father’s tent, both fair and dent,

1 47

Returning in her soul aye up and down
The words of this sudden Diomede,
His great estate, and peril of the town,
And that she was alone and had great need
Of friends' help; and thus began to breed
The cause why, the truth for to tell,
That she took fully purpose there to dwell.

1 48

The morrow came, and ghostly for to speak,
This Diomede is come unto Cressida,
And shortly, lest that ye my tale break,
So well he for himself spake and said
That all her sighs sore down he laid;
And finally, the truth for to say,
He reft her of the great of all her pain.

1 49

And after this the story telleth us,
That she him gave the fair bay steed,
Which once he won of Troilus;
And eke a brooch (and that was little need)
That Troilus gave her, she giveth to Diomede.
And eke, the better from sorrow him to relieve,
She made him wear a pennon of her sleeve.

1 50

I find also in the stories elsewhere,
When deep wounded once was Diomede
By Troilus, she wept many a tear;
When that she saw his wide wounds bleed;
And that she took to keep him good heed,
And for to heal him of his sorrows smart,
Men say that she gave him there her heart.

1 51

And yet the story also telleth us,
There made never woman more woe
Than she, when that she falsed Troilus.
She said, “Alas! for now is clean ago
My name of troth in love, for ever-mo!
For I have falsed one, the gentlest
That ever was, and one the worthiest!

1 52

Alas, of me, unto the world’s end,
Shall neither be written nor be song
No good word, for these books will me shend;
O, rolled shall I be on many a tongue;
Throughout the world my bell shall be rung;
And women most will hate me of all.
Alas, that such a case on me should fall!

1 53

And they will say, in scorning of all this,
That I dishonored them, alack the day!
Though I were not first that did amiss,
That will not wipe the blot of shame away.
But since what’s done must so for ever stay,
And since my former guilt I can’t undo,
To Diomede at least I shall be true.

1 54

But Troilus, since I can do no more,
And since our paths henceforth must separate,
May heaven to its favor thee restore!
O Troilus, the best and gentlest mate
Who ever his heart to love did consecrate,
What other love can stand in thy dear stead?”

She brake and bitter tears she then forth shed.

1 55

“I am sure that I shall hate you never,
A friend’s love you shall have at least of me,
And my good word, though I should live forever.
And truly I should grieve if I should see
You ever fall into adversity.
That you are guiltless, no one need me tell;
God’s will be done! And thus I say farewell!”

1 56

How long a time it was that lay between
Ere she forsook him for bold Diomede,
No author tells, so far as I have seen,
And no man, let him never so widely read,
Shall find a further record of this deed;
But Diomede, though quick enough to woo,
Before he won her yet had more to do.

1 57

Nor shall I now this woman further chide
Than from her simple story doth arise;
Her name, alas, is published far and wide,
Her guilt is plain enough to all men’s eyes.

> And eek from every companye he fledde;  
> This was the lyf that al the tyme he ledde.

> He so defet was, that no maner man

> And with his ire he thus himselfen shente.  
> And who-so axed him wher-of him smerte,  
> He seyde, his harm was al about his herte.
